<p>The <b>Waldorf Astoria New York</b> is a luxury hotel in <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>. The hotel has been housed in two historic landmark buildings in New York. The first, bearing the <a href="/wiki/Waldorf%E2%80%93Astoria_(New_York,_1893)" title="Waldorf–Astoria (New York, 1893)">same name</a>, was built in two stages, as the Waldorf Hotel and the Astor Hotel, which accounts for its dual name. That original site was situated on <a href="/wiki/Astor_family" title="Astor family">Astor family</a> properties along <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Avenue" title="Fifth Avenue">Fifth Avenue</a>, opened in 1893, and designed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_J._Hardenbergh" title="Henry J. Hardenbergh">Henry J. Hardenbergh</a>. It was demolished in 1929 to make way for the construction of the <a href="/wiki/Empire_State_Building" title="Empire State Building">Empire State Building</a>. The present building, at 301 <a href="/wiki/Park_Avenue" title="Park Avenue">Park Avenue</a> between 49th and 50th Streets in <a href="/wiki/Midtown_Manhattan" title="Midtown Manhattan">Midtown Manhattan</a>, is a 47-story 190.5 m (625 ft) <a href="/wiki/Art_Deco" title="Art Deco">Art Deco</a> landmark designed by architects <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Schultze_and_Weaver" title="Schultze and Weaver">Schultze and Weaver</a>, which was completed in 1931. The current hotel was the world's tallest hotel from 1931 until 1963, when it was surpassed by Moscow's <a href="/wiki/Radisson_Royal_Hotel,_Moscow" title="Radisson Royal Hotel, Moscow">Hotel Ukraina</a> by 7 metres (23 ft). An icon of glamour and luxury,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlippin201134_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlippin201134-5">[5]</a></sup> the current Waldorf Astoria is one of the world's most prestigious and best known hotels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBernardo201040_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBernardo201040-6">[6]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waldorf_Astoria_Hotels_and_Resorts" title="Waldorf Astoria Hotels and Resorts">Waldorf Astoria Hotels and Resorts</a> is a division of Hilton Hotels, and a portfolio of high-end properties around the world, now operate under the name, including New York.</p>
<p>From its inception, the Waldorf Astoria gained international renown for its lavish dinner parties and galas, often at the center of political and business conferences and fundraising schemes involving the rich and famous. Particularly after World War II it played a significant role in world politics and the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, culminating in the controversial <a href="/wiki/World_Peace_Council#History.23Wroclaw_1948_and_New_York_1949" title="World Peace Council">World Peace Conference of March 1949</a> at the hotel, in which Stalinism was widely denounced. <a href="/wiki/Conrad_Hilton" title="Conrad Hilton">Conrad Hilton</a> acquired management rights to the hotel on October 12, 1949, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hilton_Hotels_Corporation" title="Hilton Hotels Corporation">Hilton Hotels Corporation</a> finally bought the hotel outright in 1972. It underwent a $150 million renovation by <a href="/wiki/Lee_Jablin" title="Lee Jablin">Lee Jablin</a> in the 1980s and early 1990s, and in October 2014 it was announced that the <a href="/wiki/Anbang_Insurance_Group" title="Anbang Insurance Group">Anbang Insurance Group</a> of China had purchased the Waldorf Astoria New York for US$1.95 billion, making it the most expensive hotel ever sold.</p>
<p>The Waldorf Astoria and Towers has a total of 1,413 hotel rooms as of 2014. In 2009, when it had 1,416 rooms, the main hotel had 1,235 single and double rooms and 208 mini suites, while the Waldorf Towers, from the 28th floor up to the 42nd, had 181 rooms, of which 115 were suites, with one to four bedrooms. Several of the luxury suites are named after celebrities who lived or stayed in them such as The <a href="/wiki/Cole_Porter" title="Cole Porter">Cole Porter</a> Suite, The Royal Suite, named after the <a href="/wiki/Edward_VIII" title="Edward VIII">Duke</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wallis_Simpson" title="Wallis Simpson">Duchess of Windsor</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Douglas_MacArthur" title="Douglas MacArthur">MacArthur</a> Suite and the <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Churchill</a> Suite. The most expensive room, the Presidential Suite, is designed with Georgian-style furniture to emulate that of the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>. It was the residence of <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">Herbert Hoover</a> from his retirement for over 30 years, and <a href="/wiki/Frank_Sinatra" title="Frank Sinatra">Frank Sinatra</a> kept a suite at the Waldorf from 1979 until 1988. The hotel has three main restaurants, <i>Peacock Alley</i>, <i>The Bull and Bear Steak House</i>, and <i>La Chine</i>, a new Chinese restaurant that replaced <i>Oscar's Brasserie</i> in late 2015. <i>Sir Harry's Bar</i>, named after British explorer Sir <a href="/wiki/Harry_Johnston" title="Harry Johnston">Harry Johnston</a>, is the home of the <a href="/wiki/Rob_Roy_(cocktail)" title="Rob Roy (cocktail)">Rob Roy</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Burns_(drink)" title="Bobby Burns (drink)">Bobbie Burns</a> cocktails.</p>
<p>On July 1, 2016, <a href="/wiki/Anbang_Insurance_Group" title="Anbang Insurance Group">Anbang Insurance Group</a>, the owner of the hotel, announced that it would convert some of the Waldorf's hotel rooms into condominiums. Beginning in the spring of 2017, the hotel will be closed for three years while renovations are performed on the structure. During this time, the restaurants of the Waldorf Astoria will also be closed during the remodeling and will reopen in three years along with the hotel.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Waldorf_Astoria_New_York&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The name of the hotel is ultimately derived from the town of <a href="/wiki/Walldorf" title="Walldorf">Walldorf</a> in Germany, the ancestral home of the prominent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German-American" title="German-American">German-American</a> <a href="/wiki/Astor_family" title="Astor family">Astor family</a> that originated there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEmmerich20137_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEmmerich20137-7">[7]</a></sup> The hotel was originally known as The Waldorf-Astoria with a single hyphen, as recalled by a popular expression and song, "Meet Me at the Hyphen." The sign was changed to a <a href="/wiki/Double_hyphen" title="Double hyphen">double hyphen</a>, looking similar to an <a href="/wiki/Equals_sign" title="Equals sign">equals sign</a>, by Conrad Hilton when he purchased the hotel in 1949.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwardian_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edwardian-8">[8]</a></sup> The double hyphen visually represents "Peacock Alley", the hallway between the two hotels that once stood where the Empire State building now stands today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchwartz2009103_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchwartz2009103-9">[9]</a></sup> The use of the double hyphen was discontinued by parent company Hilton in 2009, shortly after the introduction of the <b>Waldorf Astoria Hotels &amp; Resorts</b> chain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The hotel has since been known as the <b>Waldorf Astoria New York</b>, without any hyphen, though this is sometimes shortened to the <b>Waldorf Astoria</b>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Waldorf_Astoria_New_York&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:427px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Waldorf_and_The_Astoria_Hotels,_New_York_City_c1915.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="819" data-file-width="1466" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/The_Waldorf_and_The_Astoria_Hotels%2C_New_York_City_c1915.jpg/425px-The_Waldorf_and_The_Astoria_Hotels%2C_New_York_City_c1915.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/The_Waldorf_and_The_Astoria_Hotels%2C_New_York_City_c1915.jpg/638px-The_Waldorf_and_The_Astoria_Hotels%2C_New_York_City_c1915.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/The_Waldorf_and_The_Astoria_Hotels%2C_New_York_City_c1915.jpg/850px-The_Waldorf_and_The_Astoria_Hotels%2C_New_York_City_c1915.jpg 2x" width="425"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Waldorf_and_The_Astoria_Hotels,_New_York_City_c1915.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Engraved vignettes of the original separate hotels</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Original_buildings">Original buildings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Waldorf_Astoria_New_York&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Original buildings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Waldorf%E2%80%93Astoria_(New_York,_1893)" title="Waldorf–Astoria (New York, 1893)">Waldorf–Astoria (New York, 1893)</a></div>
<p>The original hotel started as two hotels on <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Avenue" title="Fifth Avenue">Fifth Avenue</a> built by feuding relatives. The first hotel, the 13-story, 450-room Waldorf Hotel, designed by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Janeway_Hardenbergh" title="Henry Janeway Hardenbergh">Henry Janeway Hardenbergh</a> in the German Renaissance style,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHirsh199761_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHirsh199761-11">[11]</a></sup> was opened on March 13, 1893 at the corner of Fifth Avenue and 33rd Street, on the site where millionaire developer <a href="/wiki/William_Waldorf_Astor,_1st_Viscount_Astor" title="William Waldorf Astor, 1st Viscount Astor">William Waldorf Astor</a> had his mansion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199120_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199120-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-history-13">[13]</a></sup> The original hotel stood 225 feet (69 m) high, with a frontage of about 100 feet (30 m) on Fifth Avenue, with an area of 69,475 square feet (6,454.4 m<sup>2</sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991132_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991132-14">[14]</a></sup> The original hotel was described as having a "lofty stone and brick exterior", which was "animated by an effusion of balconies, alcoves, arcades, and loggias beneath a tile roof bedecked with gables and turrets".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorrison201411_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorrison201411-15">[15]</a></sup> William Astor, motivated in part by a dispute with his aunt <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caroline_Webster_Schermerhorn_Astor" title="Caroline Webster Schermerhorn Astor">Caroline Webster Schermerhorn Astor</a>, had built the Waldorf Hotel next door to her house, on the site of his father's mansion. The hotel was built to the specifications of founding proprietor <a href="/wiki/George_Boldt" title="George Boldt">George Boldt</a>, who owned and operated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bellevue-Stratford_Hotel" title="Bellevue-Stratford Hotel">Bellevue-Stratford Hotel</a>, a fashionable hotel on Broad Street in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philadelphia,_Pennsylvania" title="Philadelphia, Pennsylvania">Philadelphia, Pennsylvania</a>, with his wife Louise. Boldt was described as "Mild mannered, undignified, unassuming", resembling "a typical German professor with his close-cropped beard which he kept fastidiously trimmed... and his pince-nez glasses on a black silk cord".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeifer1998204_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeifer1998204-16">[16]</a></sup> Boldt continued to own the Bellevue even after his relationship with the Astors blossomed.</p>
<p>At first, the Waldorf appeared destined for failure. It was originally a laughing stock with its high number of bathrooms and was known briefly as "Boldt's Folly" or "Astor's Folly",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> with the general perception of the palatial hotel being that it had no place in New York City.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Wealthy New Yorkers were angry because they viewed the construction of the hotel as the ruination of a good neighborhood. Business travelers found it too expensive and too far uptown for their needs. In the face of all of this, George Boldt decided that the hotel would host a benefit concert for St. Mary's Hospital for Children on its opening day. The hospital was the favorite charity of those on the Social Register. The ballroom filled with many of New York's First Families, who had paid five dollars for the concert and dinner at the Waldorf.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bishop_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bishop-19">[19]</a></sup> It soon became a major success, earning $4.5 million in its first year, exorbitant for that period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeifer1998204_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeifer1998204-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:373px;max-width:373px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:William_Waldorf_Astor.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="437" data-file-width="394" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/William_Waldorf_Astor.jpg/200px-William_Waldorf_Astor.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/William_Waldorf_Astor.jpg/300px-William_Waldorf_Astor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f0/William_Waldorf_Astor.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/William_Waldorf_Astor,_1st_Viscount_Astor" title="William Waldorf Astor, 1st Viscount Astor">William Waldorf Astor</a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:167px;max-width:167px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oscar-Tschirky.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="678" data-file-width="500" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Oscar-Tschirky.jpg/165px-Oscar-Tschirky.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Oscar-Tschirky.jpg/248px-Oscar-Tschirky.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Oscar-Tschirky.jpg/330px-Oscar-Tschirky.jpg 2x" width="165"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Oscar_Tschirky" title="Oscar Tschirky">Oscar Tschirky</a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>William Astor's construction of a hotel next to his aunt's house worsened his feud with her, but, with Boldt's assistance, Waldorf's cousin, <a href="/wiki/John_Jacob_Astor_IV" title="John Jacob Astor IV">John Jacob Astor IV</a> persuaded his mother to move uptown. On November 1, 1897, John Jacob Astor IV opened the 17-story Astoria Hotel on an adjacent site, and leased it to Boldt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-history-13">[13]</a></sup> The hotels were initially built as two separate structures, but Boldt planned the Astoria so it could be connected to the Waldorf by an alley. Peacock Alley was constructed to connect the two buildings,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199121_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199121-20">[20]</a></sup> and the hotel subsequently became known as the "Waldorf-Astoria", the largest hotel in the world at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnn_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnn-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>With a telephone in every room and first-class room service, the hotel was designed specifically to cater to the needs of socially prominent "wealthy upper crust" of New York and distinguished foreign visitors to the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBernardo201040_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBernardo201040-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III19918_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III19918-23">[23]</a></sup> The hotel became, according to author Sean Dennis Cashman, "a successful symbol of the opulence and achievement of the Astor family".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECashman1988373_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECashman1988373-24">[24]</a></sup> It was the first hotel to offer complete electricity and private bathrooms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-fact-25">[25]</a></sup> Founding proprietor Boldt, whose motto was "the guest is always right",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199118_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199118-26">[26]</a></sup> became wealthy and prominent internationally, if not so much a popular celebrity as his famous employee, <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Tschirky" title="Oscar Tschirky">Oscar Tschirky</a>, known as "Oscar of the Waldorf", <i><a href="/wiki/Ma%C3%AEtre_d%27h%C3%B4tel" title="Maître d'hôtel">maître d'hôtel</a></i> from the hotel's inauguration in 1893 until his retirement in 1943. Tschirky had arrived in the United States from Switzerland ten years prior to applying for the position at the new Waldorf and over the years grew to possess a great knowledge of cuisine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2007595_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2007595-27">[27]</a></sup> He authored <i>The Cookbook by Oscar of The Waldorf</i> (1896), a 900-page book featuring all of the popular recipes of the day, including his own, for which he garnered great acclaim, such as <a href="/wiki/Waldorf_salad" title="Waldorf salad">Waldorf salad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eggs_Benedict" title="Eggs Benedict">Eggs Benedict</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thousand_Island_dressing" title="Thousand Island dressing">Thousand Island dressing</a>, which remain popular worldwide today. James Remington McCarthy wrote in his book <i>Peacock Alley</i> that Oscar gained renown among the general public as an artist who "composed sonatas in soups, symphonies in salads, minuets in sauces, lyrics in entrees".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2007595_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2007595-27">[27]</a></sup> In 1902 Tschirky published <i>Serving a Course Dinner by Oscar of the Waldorf-Astoria</i>, a booklet that explains the intricacies of being a caterer to the American and international elite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2007595_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2007595-27">[27]</a></sup> Tschirky had an excellent memory and an encyclopedic memory of the culinary preferences of many of the guests, which further added to his popularity. In 1937, for instance, he recalled the opening night and notable people present at the old Waldorf, a guest at the old building known to the public as <a href="/wiki/Buffalo_Bill" title="Buffalo Bill">Buffalo Bill</a>, and spoke at length about the planning for the <a href="/wiki/Panama_Canal" title="Panama Canal">Panama Canal</a> that took place at the Waldorf-Astoria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:377px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NY_Octagon_Room_Waldof_Astoria_Hotel_1893.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="368" data-file-width="800" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/NY_Octagon_Room_Waldof_Astoria_Hotel_1893.jpg/375px-NY_Octagon_Room_Waldof_Astoria_Hotel_1893.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/NY_Octagon_Room_Waldof_Astoria_Hotel_1893.jpg/563px-NY_Octagon_Room_Waldof_Astoria_Hotel_1893.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/NY_Octagon_Room_Waldof_Astoria_Hotel_1893.jpg/750px-NY_Octagon_Room_Waldof_Astoria_Hotel_1893.jpg 2x" width="375"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NY_Octagon_Room_Waldof_Astoria_Hotel_1893.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The original hotel's Octagon Room, 1893</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Waldorf gained significant renown internationally for its fundraising dinners and balls, regularly attracting notables of the day such as <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Carnegie" title="Andrew Carnegie">Andrew Carnegie</a>, who became a fixture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENasaw2007841_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENasaw2007841-30">[30]</a></sup> Banquets were often held in the ballroom for esteemed figures and international royalty. The Waldorf Astoria was influential in advancing the status of women, who were admitted singly without escorts. George Boldt's wife, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Louise_Kehrer_Boldt&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Louise Kehrer Boldt (page does not exist)">Louise Kehrer Boldt</a>, was influential in evolving the idea of the grand urban hotel as a social center, particularly in making it appealing to women as a venue for social events. On February 11, 1899, Oscar hosted a lavish dinner reception that the <i>New York Herald Tribune</i> cited as the city's costliest dinner at the time. Some $250 was spent per guest, with bluepoint oysters, green turtle soup, lobster, ruddy duck and blue raspberries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199132_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199132-31">[31]</a></sup> One article that year claimed that at any one time the hotel had $7 million worth of valuables locked in the safe, testament to the wealth of its guests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> In 1902 a lavish dinner was organized for <a href="/wiki/Prince_Henry_of_Prussia_(1862%E2%80%931929)" title="Prince Henry of Prussia (1862–1929)">Prince Henry of Prussia</a>, and in 1909 banquets, attended by hundreds, were organized for Arctic explorer <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Cook" title="Frederick Cook">Frederick Cook</a> in September and <a href="/wiki/Elbert_Henry_Gary" title="Elbert Henry Gary">Elbert Henry Gary</a>, a founder of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Steel" title="US Steel">US Steel</a>, the following month.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_inquiry_into_the_sinking_of_the_RMS_Titanic" title="United States Senate inquiry into the sinking of the RMS Titanic">United States Senate inquiry into the sinking of the RMS <i>Titanic</i></a> was opened at the hotel on April 19, 1912 and continued there for some time in the Myrtle Room,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuntzSmith199877_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuntzSmith199877-35">[35]</a></sup> before moving on to <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> John Jacob Astor IV was one of the people who perished on its ill-fated journey. Seven senators were present on the subcommittee, including <a href="/wiki/William_Alden_Smith" title="William Alden Smith">William Alden Smith</a> (Republican, Michigan) as chair, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jonathan_Bourne,_Jr." title="Jonathan Bourne, Jr.">Jonathan Bourne</a> (Republican, Oregon), <a href="/wiki/Theodore_E._Burton" title="Theodore E. Burton">Theodore E. Burton</a> (Republican, Ohio), <a href="/wiki/Duncan_U._Fletcher" title="Duncan U. Fletcher">Duncan U. Fletcher</a> (Democrat, Florida), <a href="/wiki/Francis_G._Newlands" title="Francis G. Newlands">Francis G. Newlands</a> (Democrat, Nevada), <a href="/wiki/George_Clement_Perkins" title="George Clement Perkins">George Clement Perkins</a> (Republican, California), and <a href="/wiki/Furnifold_McLendel_Simmons" title="Furnifold McLendel Simmons">Furnifold McLendel Simmons</a> (Democrat, North Carolina).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TIP1_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-TIP1-37">[37]</a></sup> The composition of the subcommittee was carefully chosen to represent the conservative, moderate and liberal wings of the two parties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWard2012146_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWard2012146-38">[38]</a></sup></p>

</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:266px;max-width:266px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Women_in_Waldorf-Astoria.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="584" data-file-width="454" height="340" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Women_in_Waldorf-Astoria.jpg/264px-Women_in_Waldorf-Astoria.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Women_in_Waldorf-Astoria.jpg/396px-Women_in_Waldorf-Astoria.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/13/Women_in_Waldorf-Astoria.jpg 2x" width="264"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Women operating <a href="/wiki/Stock_market_data_systems" title="Stock market data systems">stock market board</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Ticker_tape" title="Ticker tape">ticker tape</a> machine at the Waldorf in 1918, <a href="/wiki/United_States_home_front_during_World_War_I" title="United States home front during World War I">during World War I</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1919, restaurateur <a href="/wiki/Louis_Sherry" title="Louis Sherry">Louis Sherry</a> announced an "alliance" with the Waldorf-Astoria that involved both his candies and catering services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Although it was not disclosed at that time, at some point ownership of <a href="/wiki/Louis_Sherry_Inc." title="Louis Sherry Inc.">Louis Sherry Inc.</a> was significantly vested in "Boomer-duPont interests", a reference to Lucius M. Boomer, then chairman of the Waldorf-Astoria, and <a href="/wiki/T._Coleman_du_Pont" title="T. Coleman du Pont">T. Coleman du Pont</a>. Upon his death that year, William Waldorf Astor was reputed to have been worth £200 million, which he left in trust for his two sons Waldorf and John Jacob. His half share of the Waldorf Astoria and the Astor Hotel at the time were reported to have been worth £10 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKlein200525_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlein200525-40">[40]</a></sup> On the evening of November 15, 1926, the <a href="/wiki/NBC" title="NBC">National Broadcasting Company</a> broadcast its inaugural program from the grand ballroom of the old Waldorf-Astoria. Among the entertainers heard by radio listeners was <a href="/wiki/Will_Rogers" title="Will Rogers">Will Rogers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The network became the Red Network on January 1, 1927 when NBC launched its second network, designated the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Network" title="Blue Network">Blue Network</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> An <a href="/wiki/Competition_law" title="Competition law">antitrust suit</a> forced the sale of the Blue Network in 1942; it became the <a href="/wiki/American_Broadcasting_Company" title="American Broadcasting Company">American Broadcasting Company</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>The hotel faced stiff competition from the early 20th century, with a range of new hotels springing up in New York City such the <a href="/wiki/Hotel_Astor_(New_York_City)" title="Hotel Astor (New York City)">Hotel Astor</a> (1904), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_St._Regis_Hotel" title="The St. Regis Hotel">The St. Regis</a> (1904), <a href="/wiki/The_Knickerbocker_Hotel_(Manhattan)" title="The Knickerbocker Hotel (Manhattan)">The Knickerbocker</a> (1906), and the <a href="/wiki/Savoy-Plaza_Hotel" title="Savoy-Plaza Hotel">Savoy-Plaza Hotel</a> (1927).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlanke2002121_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlanke2002121-44">[44]</a></sup> By the 1920s, the hotel was becoming dated, and the elegant social life of New York had moved much farther north than 34th Street. The Astor family finally sold the hotel to the developers of the <a href="/wiki/Empire_State_Building" title="Empire State Building">Empire State Building</a> and closed the hotel on May 3, 1929. It was demolished soon after.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-history-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:left;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_building">Current building</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Waldorf_Astoria_New_York&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Current building">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years_and_international_politics">Early years and international politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Waldorf_Astoria_New_York&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Early years and international politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The idea of a new Waldorf-Astoria hotel was based on the concept that there should be a large opulent hotel in New York for distinguished visitors. Financial backing was not difficult to get in the summer of 1929 as times were prosperous; the stock market had not yet crashed nor had the Depression arrived. However, before ground was broken for the new building, some of the investors became dubious about whether this was the right time to be investing in a luxury hotel. The land for the new hotel was formerly owned by the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Central_Railroad" title="New York Central Railroad">New York Central Railroad</a>, who had promised $10 million toward the building of the new Waldorf-Astoria. The railroad and all the other investors decided to honor their commitments and take their chances with the uncertain financial climate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sea_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-sea-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>The new building opened on Park Avenue, between East 49th and East 50th streets, on October 1, 1931. It was the tallest and largest hotel in the world at the time,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKorom2008422_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKorom2008422-47">[47]</a></sup> covering the entire block. The slender central tower became known as the <b>Waldorf Towers</b>, with its own private entrance on 50th Street, and consisted of 100 suites, about one third of which were leased as private residences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorrison2014105_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorrison2014105-48">[48]</a></sup> President <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">Herbert Hoover</a> said on the radio, broadcast from the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>: "The opening of the new Waldorf Astoria is an event in the advancement of hotels, even in New York City. It carries great tradition in national hospitality...marks the measure of nation's growth in power, in comfort and in artistry...an exhibition of courage and confidence to the whole nation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-history-13">[13]</a></sup> There were 2,000 people in the ballroom listening to this speech, but by the end of the business day, the 2,200-room hotel had only 500 occupancies. It was not until 1939 that the Waldorf-Astoria began operating at a profit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sea_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-sea-46">[46]</a></sup> Lucius Boomer continued to manage the hotel in the 1930s and 1940s, a commanding figure who Tony Rey referred to as "the greatest hotelman of his era".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199140_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199140-49">[49]</a></sup> Boomer was elected chairman of the board of the Waldorf-Astoria Corporation on February 20, 1945, a position he held until his death in July 1947.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199146-7_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199146-7-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>Like the original hotel, from its inception, the Waldorf Astoria gained worldwide renown for its glamorous dinner parties and galas, often at the center of political and business conferences and fundraising schemes. Author Ward Morehouse III has referred to the Waldorf Astoria as "comparable to great national institutions" and a "living symbol deep within our collective consciousness".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991259_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991259-52">[52]</a></sup> It had the "greatest banquet department in the world" at the time according to restaurateur <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tom_Margittai&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tom Margittai (page does not exist)">Tom Margittai</a>, with the centre of activity being the Grand Ballroom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991215_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991215-53">[53]</a></sup> On August 3, 1932, some 200 people representing the "cream of New York's literary world" attended the Waldorf Astoria to honor <a href="/wiki/Pearl_S._Buck" title="Pearl S. Buck">Pearl S. Buck</a>, the author of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Good_Earth" title="The Good Earth">The Good Earth</a></i>, which was the best-selling novel in the United States in 1931 and 1932.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarker200747_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarker200747-54">[54]</a></sup> One dinner alone, a relatively "small dinner" attended by some 50 people in June 1946, raised over $250,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchweber2009115_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchweber2009115-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>The hotel played a considerable role in the emerging <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> and international relations during the post-war years, staging numerous events and conferences. On March 15, 1946, <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> attended a welcoming dinner at the hotel given by Governor <a href="/wiki/Thomas_E._Dewey" title="Thomas E. Dewey">Thomas E. Dewey</a>, ten days after making his famous <a href="/wiki/Iron_Curtain" title="Iron Curtain">Iron Curtain</a> speech,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarbutt19883_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarbutt19883-56">[56]</a></sup> and from November 4 to December 12, 1946, the <a href="/wiki/Big_Four_Conference" title="Big Four Conference">Big Four Conference</a> was held in <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B8rgine_Boomer" title="Jørgine Boomer">Jørgine Boomer</a>'s apartment on the 37th floor of the Towers between the United States, Britain, France and the Soviet Union to discuss the future of Eastern Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991176.2C_185_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991176.2C_185-57">[57]</a></sup> On November 24, 1947, 48 prominent figures of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hollywood_film_industry" title="Hollywood film industry">Hollywood film industry</a>, including various film executives such as <a href="/wiki/Louis_B._Mayer" title="Louis B. Mayer">Louis B. Mayer</a> of <a href="/wiki/Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer" title="Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer">Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harry_Cohn" title="Harry Cohn">Harry Cohn</a> of <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Pictures" title="Columbia Pictures">Columbia Pictures</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spyros_Skouras" title="Spyros Skouras">Spyros Skouras</a> of <a href="/wiki/20th_Century_Fox" title="20th Century Fox">20th Century Fox</a> and <a href="/wiki/Albert_Warner" title="Albert Warner">Albert Warner</a> of <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros." title="Warner Bros.">Warner Bros.</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eric_Johnston" title="Eric Johnston">Eric Johnston</a>, the head of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Association_of_Motion_Picture_Producers" title="Association of Motion Picture Producers">Association of Motion Picture Producers</a>, met at the Waldorf Astoria and discussed what would become the <a href="/wiki/Waldorf_Statement" title="Waldorf Statement">Waldorf Statement</a>, banning people with Communist beliefs or tendencies from the Hollywood film industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBresler2004169_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBresler2004169-58">[58]</a></sup> The Statement was a response to the <a href="/wiki/Contempt_of_Congress" title="Contempt of Congress">contempt of Congress</a> charges against the so-called "<a href="/wiki/Hollywood_blacklist#The_Hollywood_Ten" title="Hollywood blacklist">Hollywood Ten</a>".</p>
<p>On June 21, 1948 a press conference at the hotel introduced the <a href="/wiki/LP_record" title="LP record">LP record</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> From March 27 to 29, 1949, the Cultural and Scientific Conference for World Peace, also known as the Waldorf World Peace Conference, was held at the hotel to discuss the emerging <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> and the growing divide between the US and the Soviet Union. The conference came at time when there was deep anti-Communist sentiment and suspicion of the Soviet Union in the United States, following the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Blockade" title="Berlin Blockade">Berlin Blockade</a> and the <a href="/wiki/1948_Czechoslovak_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1948 Czechoslovak coup d'état">Czechoslovak coup d'état</a> the previous year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPorter2010149_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPorter2010149-60">[60]</a></sup> The event was organized by the struggling <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Communist_Party" title="American Communist Party">American Communist Party</a>, but was sponsored by many individuals who were not Stalinists such as <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Bernstein" title="Leonard Bernstein">Leonard Bernstein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marlon_Brando" title="Marlon Brando">Marlon Brando</a>, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Copland" title="Aaron Copland">Aaron Copland</a>, with the intention of promoting peace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESorin2002109_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESorin2002109-61">[61]</a></sup> The conference was attended by the likes of Soviet Foreign Minister <a href="/wiki/Andrey_Vyshinsky" title="Andrey Vyshinsky">Andrey Vyshinsky</a>, composer and pianist <a href="/wiki/Dmitri_Shostakovich" title="Dmitri Shostakovich">Dmitri Shostakovich</a> and writer <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Fadeyev_(writer)" title="Alexander Fadeyev (writer)">Alexsander Fadeyev</a>. Tension mounted during the controversial event, and culminated when Shostakovich, in front of a crowd of some 800 people, launched a scathing attack on western civilization, remarking that "a small clique of hatemongers was preparing world public opinion for the transition from cold war to outright aggression".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarroll200625_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarroll200625-62">[62]</a></sup> The event was picketed in a counter-attack by anti-Stalinists running under the banner of "<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=America_for_Intellectual_Freedom&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="America for Intellectual Freedom (page does not exist)">America for Intellectual Freedom</a>" (AIF), and prominent individuals such as <a href="/wiki/Irving_Howe" title="Irving Howe">Irving Howe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dwight_Macdonald" title="Dwight Macdonald">Dwight Macdonald</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mary_McCarthy_(author)" title="Mary McCarthy (author)">Mary McCarthy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Lowell" title="Robert Lowell">Robert Lowell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norman_Mailer" title="Norman Mailer">Norman Mailer</a> publicly denounced Stalinism at the hotel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESorin2002109_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESorin2002109-61">[61]</a></sup> In 1954, Israeli statesman and archaeologist <a href="/wiki/Yigael_Yadin" title="Yigael Yadin">Yigael Yadin</a> met secretly with the <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox_Church" title="Syriac Orthodox Church">Syriac Orthodox</a> Archbishop <a href="/wiki/Mar_Samuel" title="Mar Samuel">Mar Samuel</a> in the basement of the Waldorf-Astoria Hotel to negotiate the purchase of four <a href="/wiki/Dead_Sea_Scrolls" title="Dead Sea Scrolls">Dead Sea Scrolls</a> for <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>. The scrolls were kept in a vault at the Waldorf-Astoria branch of New York's <a href="/wiki/Chemical_Bank" title="Chemical Bank">Chemical Bank</a>. At the request of the Israeli government, respected biblical scholar Dr. <a href="/wiki/Harry_Orlinsky" title="Harry Orlinsky">Harry Orlinsky</a> examined the scrolls and verified their authenticity; Yadin paid $250,000 for all four.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scrolls_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-scrolls-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-buy_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-buy-66">[note 1]</a></sup> Restaurateur <a href="/wiki/George_Lang_(restaurateur)" title="George Lang (restaurateur)">George Lang</a> began working at the Waldorf Astoria Hotel in 1955, and on December 13, 1955 he helped organize the American Theatre Wing's First Night Ball to celebrate <a href="/wiki/Helen_Hayes" title="Helen Hayes">Helen Hayes</a>'s 50th year in show business.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991125_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991125-67">[66]</a></sup> He did much to organize dinners at the Waldorf to assist Hungarian issues and relief. On one occasion an event was attended by the likes of <a href="/wiki/Edward_G._Robinson" title="Edward G. Robinson">Edward G. Robinson</a> and pianist Doklady and some $60,000 was raised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991126_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991126-68">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="April_in_Paris_Ball">April in Paris Ball</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Waldorf_Astoria_New_York&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: April in Paris Ball">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/April_in_Paris_Ball" title="April in Paris Ball">April in Paris Ball</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Elsa_Maxwell_Marilyn_Monroe_April_in_Paris_Ball_1957.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="997" data-file-width="934" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Elsa_Maxwell_Marilyn_Monroe_April_in_Paris_Ball_1957.jpg/220px-Elsa_Maxwell_Marilyn_Monroe_April_in_Paris_Ball_1957.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Elsa_Maxwell_Marilyn_Monroe_April_in_Paris_Ball_1957.jpg/330px-Elsa_Maxwell_Marilyn_Monroe_April_in_Paris_Ball_1957.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Elsa_Maxwell_Marilyn_Monroe_April_in_Paris_Ball_1957.jpg/440px-Elsa_Maxwell_Marilyn_Monroe_April_in_Paris_Ball_1957.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Elsa_Maxwell_Marilyn_Monroe_April_in_Paris_Ball_1957.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Marilyn_Monroe" title="Marilyn Monroe">Marilyn Monroe</a> with organizer <a href="/wiki/Elsa_Maxwell" title="Elsa Maxwell">Elsa Maxwell</a> at the 1957 April in Paris Ball</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/April_in_Paris_Ball" title="April in Paris Ball">April in Paris Ball</a> was an annual gala event whose mission was to improve <a href="/wiki/France%E2%80%93United_States_relations" title="France–United States relations">Franco–American relations</a>, to share cultures, and to help provide assistance to US and French charities, aside from commemorating the 2000th anniversary of the founding of Paris.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991226_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991226-69">[68]</a></sup> It was established by <a href="/wiki/Claude_Philippe" title="Claude Philippe">Claude Philippe</a>, the hotel banquet manager, in 1952. While the hotel's management handled invitations and publicity, other details were coordinated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Socialites" title="Socialites">socialites</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETime_Inc196090_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETime_Inc196090-70">[69]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Elsa_Maxwell" title="Elsa Maxwell">Elsa Maxwell</a> was given the primary responsibility in organizing it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAvery201050_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAvery201050-71">[70]</a></sup> It was initially held annually in April, but according to Ann Vaccaro, former executive director of the ball, it was changed to October because "Mr. Philippe decided that because there are so many balls in the spring he would make it in October".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991225_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991225-72">[71]</a></sup> After being changed to October, it often marked the start of the US fall social season. It was staged in the Grand Ballroom at the Waldorf for eight years before moving to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hotel_Astor" title="Hotel Astor">Hotel Astor</a> in 1960, the <a href="/wiki/Seventh_Regiment_Armory" title="Seventh Regiment Armory">Seventh Regiment Armory</a> in 1961, and other venues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991235_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991235-73">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>The ball was designed to cater for "very, very high-class people" according to Vaccaro.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991227_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991227-74">[73]</a></sup> Raffle tickets cost US$100 per person and offered opulent prizes such as a US$5000 bracelet and other jewels, expensive furs, perfumes and even cars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991227_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991227-74">[73]</a></sup> In the 1960 event, prizes given included a <a href="/wiki/Ford_Thunderbird" title="Ford Thunderbird">Ford Thunderbird</a> car, a <a href="/wiki/Chinchilla" title="Chinchilla">Chinchilla</a> coat, a <a href="/wiki/Renault_Dauphine" title="Renault Dauphine">Renault Dauphine</a>, a TV <a href="/wiki/High_fidelity" title="High fidelity">Hi-Fi</a> system, an electric typewriter, 25 cases of expensive French wines, original paintings and porcelains, jewels, clocks, evening bags and a pedigree poodle; guests were given gift boxes containing gold key rings and jewelry, champagne and brandy, Maxim ashtrays, pipes, silver bottle openers, hats and scarves, and flowers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991235_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991235-73">[72]</a></sup> Every guest was said to have gone home with at least one gift in return. In the 1979 event, some US$106,000 worth of prizes were given out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991227_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991227-74">[73]</a></sup> Over its history, the ball, which was exempt from tax, earned millions of dollars, which went primarily to over 20 American charities such as the <a href="/wiki/American_Cancer_Society" title="American Cancer Society">American Cancer Society</a>, with 15 to 20% going towards French charities. A staff of three people were paid full-time throughout the year to organize it. Of the expenses of the ball, founder Philippe stated "We charge the most, give the most, and make the most – it's a success formula".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991226_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991226-69">[68]</a></sup> Bernard F. Gimbel served as chief treasurer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991227_74-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991227-74">[73]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marlene_Dietrich_April_in_Paris_Ball_1959.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="738" data-file-width="511" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Marlene_Dietrich_April_in_Paris_Ball_1959.jpg/170px-Marlene_Dietrich_April_in_Paris_Ball_1959.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Marlene_Dietrich_April_in_Paris_Ball_1959.jpg/255px-Marlene_Dietrich_April_in_Paris_Ball_1959.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Marlene_Dietrich_April_in_Paris_Ball_1959.jpg/340px-Marlene_Dietrich_April_in_Paris_Ball_1959.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marlene_Dietrich_April_in_Paris_Ball_1959.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Marlene_Dietrich" title="Marlene Dietrich">Marlene Dietrich</a> at the April in Paris Ball in 1959</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Paris Ball became a notable event in the annual calendar during the 1950s, with one early show featuring a "three-hour spectacular of five tableaux, directed by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Stuart_Chaney&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Stuart Chaney (page does not exist)">Stuart Chaney</a>", [depicting] a twelfth century scene of troubadours at the court of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eleanor_of_Aquitane" title="Eleanor of Aquitane">Eleanor of Aquitane</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_VIII" title="Henry VIII">Henry VIII</a>'s meeting at the <a href="/wiki/Field_of_the_Cloth_of_Gold" title="Field of the Cloth of Gold">Field of the Cloth of Gold</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louis_XIV" title="Louis XIV">Louis XIV</a> at <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Versailles" title="Palace of Versailles">Versailles</a>, and a fashion show of forty creations by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dior" title="Dior">Dior</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Fath" title="Jacques Fath">Fath</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Balmain" title="Pierre Balmain">Balmain</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Desses" title="Desses">Desses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Givenchy" title="Givenchy">Givenchy</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991226_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991226-69">[68]</a></sup> French stars <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Juliette_Greco" title="Juliette Greco">Juliette Greco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean_Sablon" title="Jean Sablon">Jean Sablon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beatrice_Lillie" title="Beatrice Lillie">Beatrice Lillie</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Loder_(actor)" title="John Loder (actor)">John Loder</a> and many others were flown over for the ball. The 1957 event was attended by some 1300 guests, including the <a href="/wiki/Edward_VIII" title="Edward VIII">Duke</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wallis_Simpson" title="Wallis Simpson">Duchess of Windsor</a>, Senator <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a>, his wife, <a href="/wiki/Jacqueline_Kennedy_Onassis" title="Jacqueline Kennedy Onassis">Jackie</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Marilyn_Monroe" title="Marilyn Monroe">Marilyn Monroe</a>, who paid $100 each and donated $130,000 to charities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETime_Inc1957201_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETime_Inc1957201-75">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKing2011427_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKing2011427-76">[75]</a></sup> The following year, the ballroom was decorated with 30 feet (9.1 m) high chestnut trees, earning US$170,000 for charities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETime_Inc1958137_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETime_Inc1958137-77">[76]</a></sup> The final ball to be hosted in the hotel was held on April 10, 1959, with the main theme being the Parisian circus of the 18th century. Genuine circus costumes from the period were flown over from France, and the ball was attended by <a href="/wiki/Marlene_Dietrich" title="Marlene Dietrich">Marlene Dietrich</a>, who performed two <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Chevalier" title="Maurice Chevalier">Maurice Chevalier</a> songs, wearing a top hat, trousers, a waistcoat and white gloves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991230_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991230-78">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_history">Later history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Waldorf_Astoria_New_York&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Later history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On May 6, 1963, <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine celebrated its 40th anniversary at the hotel. The event was attended by some 1500 celebrities,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991254_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991254-79">[78]</a></sup> including General <a href="/wiki/Douglas_MacArthur" title="Douglas MacArthur">Douglas MacArthur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean_Monnet" title="Jean Monnet">Jean Monnet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cabot_Lodge" title="Henry Cabot Lodge">Henry Cabot Lodge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bob_Hope" title="Bob Hope">Bob Hope</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joe_Louis" title="Joe Louis">Joe Louis</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_O._Selznick" title="David O. Selznick">David O. Selznick</a>, Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Dean_Rusk" title="Dean Rusk">Dean Rusk</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edward_Kennedy" title="Edward Kennedy">Edward Kennedy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Ford_II" title="Henry Ford II">Henry Ford II</a>, and many others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991218_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991218-80">[79]</a></sup> In 1968, British rock band <a href="/wiki/The_Who" title="The Who">The Who</a> checked into the hotel, where they encountered difficulties with the staff of the Waldorf Astoria. Due to the band's reputation for trashing their hotel rooms and rowdy behavior, the Waldorf demanded that they pay cash up front. However, following their gig, the band members were refused access to their hotel rooms, where their luggage was being kept. Tony Fletcher, in his biography on <a href="/wiki/Keith_Moon" title="Keith Moon">Keith Moon</a>, claims that Moon challenged the staff and blew the door to their room off the hinges with his cherry bombs and retrieved their luggage, which prompted The Who to be shown the door and banned from the hotel for life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFletcher2010376_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFletcher2010376-81">[80]</a></sup> However, clearly the ban was later revoked as they performed at the <a href="/wiki/Rock_and_Roll_Hall_of_Fame" title="Rock and Roll Hall of Fame">Rock and Roll Hall of Fame</a> Induction at Waldorf on January 17, 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Conrad_Hilton_(croped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="725" data-file-width="552" height="289" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Conrad_Hilton_%28croped%29.jpg/220px-Conrad_Hilton_%28croped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Conrad_Hilton_%28croped%29.jpg/330px-Conrad_Hilton_%28croped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Conrad_Hilton_%28croped%29.jpg/440px-Conrad_Hilton_%28croped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Conrad_Hilton_(croped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Conrad_Hilton" title="Conrad Hilton">Conrad Hilton</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Soon after the opening of the hotel in 1931, hotelier <a href="/wiki/Conrad_Hilton" title="Conrad Hilton">Conrad Hilton</a>, almost bankrupt at the time, reportedly cut out a photograph of the hotel from a magazine and wrote across it, "The Greatest of Them All".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDana2011227_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDana2011227-83">[82]</a></sup> He acquired management rights to the hotel on October 12, 1949.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaraborrelli2014118_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaraborrelli2014118-84">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hilton_Hotels_Corporation" title="Hilton Hotels Corporation">Hilton Hotels Corporation</a> finally bought the hotel outright in 1972.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1970s, the Waldorf Astoria continued to play an important role in international politics, particularly between the US and the Middle East. In November 1974, a "20-car motorcade, with eight shotgun-toting police marksmen aboard in bullet-proof vests" brought Palestinian <a href="/wiki/Fatah" title="Fatah">Fatah</a> party leader <a href="/wiki/Farouk_Kaddoumi" title="Farouk Kaddoumi">Farouk Kaddoumi</a> to the Waldorf from <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy_International_Airport" title="John F. Kennedy International Airport">John F. Kennedy International Airport</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup> The Waldorf was on red alert, and <a href="/wiki/German_Shepherd" title="German Shepherd">German Shepherd</a> sniffer dogs were brought in prior to his arrival to look for possible bombs. 15 suites of the hotel were reserved for the <a href="/wiki/Palestine_Liberation_Organization" title="Palestine Liberation Organization">Palestine Liberation Organization</a> (PLO) Arab delegation. The following month, President Ford met with <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Rockefeller" title="Nelson Rockefeller">Nelson Rockefeller</a> after he was voted <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_the_United_States" title="Vice President of the United States">Vice President</a>, and a 90-minute press conference was held in a suite in the hotel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup> In November 1975, the US government insisted that PLO leader <a href="/wiki/Yasser_Arafat" title="Yasser Arafat">Yasser Arafat</a> stay at the Waldorf during his visit to America, against the wishes of the hotel staff; security was stepped up severely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991185-6_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991185-6-89">[88]</a></sup> On August 12, 1981, <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a> unveiled its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IBM_5150" title="IBM 5150">Personal Computer</a> in a press conference at the Waldorf Astoria,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittman1987155_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittman1987155-90">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup> and in 1985, the <a href="/wiki/National_Basketball_Association" title="National Basketball Association">NBA</a> held its first-ever draft lottery between non-playoff teams at the Starlight Room. The lottery was for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1985_NBA_Draft" title="1985 NBA Draft">1985 NBA Draft</a> in which <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Ewing" title="Patrick Ewing">Patrick Ewing</a> was the consensus number one pick.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFizel201241_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFizel201241-92">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lee_Jablin" title="Lee Jablin">Lee Jablin</a>, of Harman Jablin Architects, fully renovated and upgraded the historical property to its original grandeur during the mid-1980s through the mid-1990s in a $150 million renovation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199157_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199157-93">[92]</a></sup> The hotel was named an official New York City Landmark in 1993.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_13-4"><a href="#cite_note-history-13">[13]</a></sup> On May 27, 2001, the Eastern Diocese of the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Armenian_Church_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Armenian Church of America (page does not exist)">Armenian Church of America</a> had a grand banquet at the hotel to celebrate the 1700th anniversary of Armenia's conversion to Christianity, with Ambassador <a href="/wiki/Edward_Djerejian" title="Edward Djerejian">Edward Djerejian</a> as guest speaker.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup> On May 7, 2004, a press conference was held by MGM, discussing <a href="/wiki/Steve_Martin" title="Steve Martin">Steve Martin</a>'s <a href="/wiki/The_Pink_Panther_(2006_film)" title="The Pink Panther (2006 film)">The Pink Panther</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Pink_Panther" title="The Pink Panther">The Pink Panther</a></i>. The 5th Annual DGA Honors Gala was held at the Waldorf on September 29.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2006, Hilton launched <a href="/wiki/Waldorf_Astoria_Hotels_%26_Resorts" title="Waldorf Astoria Hotels &amp; Resorts">Waldorf Astoria Hotels &amp; Resorts</a>, a global luxury brand named for the iconic hotel. There are now branches of the Waldorf Astoria in Arizona, California, Florida, Hawaii, Louisiana in the United States, and abroad in France, Israel, Italy and Saudi Arabia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKnight2014923_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKnight2014923-96">[95]</a></sup> In 2006 it was reported that Hilton were considering opening a new Waldorf Astoria hotel on the <a href="/wiki/Las_Vegas_Strip" title="Las Vegas Strip">Las Vegas Strip</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup> In 2008 the Waldorf Astoria opened the Guerlain and Spa Chakra, Inc. spa at the hotel, as part of the Waldorf Astoria Collection, which offers a "body massage and facial using Guerlain's age-defying Orchidee Imperiale skincare".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Waldorf Astoria New York is a member of Historic Hotels of America, the official program of the <a href="/wiki/National_Trust_for_Historic_Preservation" title="National Trust for Historic Preservation">National Trust for Historic Preservation</a>. "The Towers of the Waldorf Astoria" continues to operate as a boutique "hotel within a hotel".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_13-5"><a href="#cite_note-history-13">[13]</a></sup> In October 2014, it was announced that the <a href="/wiki/Anbang_Insurance_Group" title="Anbang Insurance Group">Anbang Insurance Group</a>, based in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, had purchased the Waldorf Astoria New York for US$1.95 billion, making it the most expensive hotel ever sold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt_20141007_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt_20141007-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>On July 1, 2016, <a href="/wiki/Anbang_Insurance_Group" title="Anbang Insurance Group">Anbang Insurance Group</a>, the owner of the hotel, announced plans to refurbish the hotel and turn some rooms into <a href="/wiki/Condominium" title="Condominium">condominiums</a>. Some of the hotel's rooms will be turned into apartments, with the remainder of the rooms remaining hotel suites. The hotel will be closed for three years starting in the spring of 2017 for refurbishment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup> The hotel's restaurants, including <i>Peacock Alley</i>, <i>The Bull and Bear Steak House</i>, and the recently opened <i>La Chine</i>, will close along with the hotel; they will reopen when the renovation is completed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Waldorf_Astoria_New_York&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_buildings">Old buildings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Waldorf_Astoria_New_York&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Old buildings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Waldorf%E2%80%93Astoria_(New_York,_1893)#Architecture" title="Waldorf–Astoria (New York, 1893)">Waldorf–Astoria (New York, 1893) § Architecture</a></div>
<div style="box-sizing: border-box; overflow: hidden; float:right; margin:1px;">
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:368px;max-width:368px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gentlemen%27s_Cafe_Waldorf.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1137" data-file-width="1363" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Gentlemen%27s_Cafe_Waldorf.jpg/180px-Gentlemen%27s_Cafe_Waldorf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Gentlemen%27s_Cafe_Waldorf.jpg/270px-Gentlemen%27s_Cafe_Waldorf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Gentlemen%27s_Cafe_Waldorf.jpg/360px-Gentlemen%27s_Cafe_Waldorf.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a></div>

</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Waldorf_restaurant.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1135" data-file-width="1368" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Waldorf_restaurant.jpg/180px-Waldorf_restaurant.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Waldorf_restaurant.jpg/270px-Waldorf_restaurant.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Waldorf_restaurant.jpg/360px-Waldorf_restaurant.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a></div>

</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:368px;max-width:368px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Henry_IV_drawing_room_Waldorf.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1143" data-file-width="1384" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Henry_IV_drawing_room_Waldorf.jpg/180px-Henry_IV_drawing_room_Waldorf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Henry_IV_drawing_room_Waldorf.jpg/270px-Henry_IV_drawing_room_Waldorf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Henry_IV_drawing_room_Waldorf.jpg/360px-Henry_IV_drawing_room_Waldorf.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Henry IV drawing room</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Waldorf_Louis_XIV_bedroom.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1129" data-file-width="1366" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Waldorf_Louis_XIV_bedroom.jpg/180px-Waldorf_Louis_XIV_bedroom.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Waldorf_Louis_XIV_bedroom.jpg/270px-Waldorf_Louis_XIV_bedroom.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Waldorf_Louis_XIV_bedroom.jpg/360px-Waldorf_Louis_XIV_bedroom.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Louis XIV bedroom</div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The old Waldorf Hotel, built at a reported cost of about US$5million, opened on March 13, 1893 at the corner of Fifth Avenue and 33rd Street, on the site where millionaire developer <a href="/wiki/William_Waldorf_Astor,_1st_Viscount_Astor" title="William Waldorf Astor, 1st Viscount Astor">William Waldorf Astor</a> had previously built his mansion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199120_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199120-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_13-6"><a href="#cite_note-history-13">[13]</a></sup> The hotel stood 225 feet (69 m) high, about 50 feet (15 m) lower than the Astoria, with a frontage of about 100 feet (30 m) on Fifth Avenue, and a total area of 69,475 square feet (6,454.4 m<sup>2</sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991132_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991132-14">[14]</a></sup> It was a German Renaissance structure, designed by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Janeway_Hardenbergh" title="Henry Janeway Hardenbergh">Henry Janeway Hardenbergh</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKing1893218_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKing1893218-102">[101]</a></sup> with 15 public rooms and 450 guest rooms, and a further 100 rooms allocated to servants, with laundry facilities on the upper floors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorrison201413_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorrison201413-103">[102]</a></sup> <i>The New York Times</i> proclaimed the hotel a palace after it opened in 1893.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorrison201411_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorrison201411-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>The exterior featured loggias, balconies, gables, groups of chimneys, and tiled roofs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEComstock189851_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEComstock189851-104">[103]</a></sup> One of the chief features was the interior garden court, with fountains and flowers, walls of white terracotta, frescoes and stained glass. The entrance hall was built in Sienna marble, with a mosaic title floor and a coffered ceiling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorrison201413_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorrison201413-103">[102]</a></sup> The original reception desk of the Waldorf Hotel became a registration desk when it merged with the Astoria Hotel in 1897.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorrison201413_103-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorrison201413-103">[102]</a></sup> Beyond the lobby was the main corridor leading to the Empire Room, with an alcove off it containing the elevators and grand staircase. Near this was the Marie Antoinette parlor, which was used as a reception room for women. It contained 18th century antiques brought back by Boldt and his wife from an 1892 visit to Europe, including a bust of <a href="/wiki/Marie_Antoinette" title="Marie Antoinette">Marie Antoinette</a>, and an antique clock which was once owned by her. The Empire Room was the largest and most lavishly adorned room in the Waldorf, and soon after opening, it became one of the best restaurants in New York City, rivaling <i><a href="/wiki/Delmonico%27s" title="Delmonico's">Delmonico's</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sherry-Netherland" title="The Sherry-Netherland">Sherry's</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorrison201415_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorrison201415-105">[104]</a></sup> It was modeled after the grand salon in <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_II_of_Bavaria" title="Ludwig II of Bavaria">King Ludwig</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Nymphenburg_Palace" title="Nymphenburg Palace">palace</a> at <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a>, with satin hangings, upholstery and marble pillars, all of pale green, and <a href="/wiki/Frank_Crowninshield" title="Frank Crowninshield">Crowninshield</a>'s frescoes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nationalacademy.org_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-nationalacademy.org-106">[105]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Empire_style" title="Empire style">Empire</a> in style, the Waldorf's restaurant feathered columns of dark-green marble, and the pilasters that came opposite them were of <a href="/wiki/Mahogany" title="Mahogany">mahogany</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Ormolu" title="Ormolu">ormolu</a> work in the panels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThe_American_Architect_and_Building_News_Company18983_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_American_Architect_and_Building_News_Company18983-107">[106]</a></sup> The Waldorf State Apartments, consisting of nine suites, were located on the second floor. The apartments, including the Henry IV Drawing Room, the Francois V Bedroom, which was a reproduction of the room at the Palais de Fontainebleau, had their own music room and a banquet hall to seat 20, with a handsome china collection including 48 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sevres" title="Sevres">Sevres</a> plates with European portraits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorrison201422_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorrison201422-108">[107]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tmulti tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:368px;max-width:368px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px">
