</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Astoria_double_bedroom.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1143" data-file-width="1389" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Astoria_double_bedroom.jpg/180px-Astoria_double_bedroom.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Astoria_double_bedroom.jpg/270px-Astoria_double_bedroom.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Astoria_double_bedroom.jpg/360px-Astoria_double_bedroom.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Astoria double bedroom</div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The old Astoria Hotel, opened in 1897, was situated on the southeast corner of Fifth Avenue and 34th Street. It was also designed in the German Renaissance style by Henry J. Hardenbergh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEComstock189851_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEComstock189851-104">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_13-7"><a href="#cite_note-history-13">[13]</a></sup> With dimensions of 99 by 350 feet (30 m × 107 m), its height, from the floor of the sub-basement, which was 33 feet (10 m) below the street level, to the roof-line, was about 270 feet (82 m), or about 240 feet (73 m) above the street-level. It was 16 stories in height, including the four stories in the roof.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThe_American_Architect_and_Building_News_Company18983_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_American_Architect_and_Building_News_Company18983-107">[106]</a></sup> The building was constructed of stone, marble and brick, with a steel skeleton frame and modern fireproof interior construction,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEComstock189851_104-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEComstock189851-104">[103]</a></sup> and was embellished with "French Second Empire Mansard-roofed towers with iron-work cresting as well as Austrian Baroque onion-domes over corners turrets".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELashleyLynchMorrison2007102_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELashleyLynchMorrison2007102-109">[108]</a></sup> There were 25 public rooms and 550 guest rooms, with miles of corridors, vestibules and balls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEComstock189851_104-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEComstock189851-104">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>The main corridor ran the entire length of the building from east to west. To the left of it was the Astor Dining Room, fronting on Fifth Avenue, which measured 50 by 92 feet (15 m × 28 m). Great care was taken with it to faithfully reproduce the original dining room of the mansion, three floors above where it had stood,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorrison201424_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorrison201424-110">[109]</a></sup> with all of the original paneling, carpeting, drapery and fireplace mantel and Italian Renaissance pilasters and columns, carved of marble from northern Russia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEComstock189851_104-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEComstock189851-104">[103]</a></sup> On the right of the main corridor was the Garden Court of Palms, decorated in the Italian style, 88 by 57 feet (27 m × 17 m). On the first floor, at the head-of the east main staircase, was the Astor Gallery, 87 by 102 feet (27 m × 31 m), looking out on 34th Street, finished in the style of the <a href="/wiki/H%C3%B4tel_de_Soubise" title="Hôtel de Soubise">Hôtel de Soubise</a>, with a blue, gray and gold color scheme. The second floor contained a private suite of apartments at the northeast corner, with large drawing rooms, dining room, butler's pantry, hallway, three bedrooms, three maids' bedrooms and five bathrooms, all finished in old English oak. All the floors above the third were given up to suites and bedrooms up to the 14th floor. The ballroom, in the Louis XIV style, has been described as the "<a href="/wiki/Pi%C3%A8ce_de_r%C3%A9sistance" title="Pièce de résistance">pièce de résistance</a>" of the hotel, measuring 65 feet (20 m) by 95 feet (29 m) and 40 feet (12 m) (three stories) in height.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELashleyLynchMorrison2007102_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELashleyLynchMorrison2007102-109">[108]</a></sup> It had a capacity to seat 700 at banquets and 1,200 at concerts, and featured tints of ivory-gray and cream in its design.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThe_American_Architect_and_Building_News_Company18983_107-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_American_Architect_and_Building_News_Company18983-107">[106]</a></sup> On the hotel's top floor was the roof-garden, enclosed on all sides by glass, with a glass roof over. It was furnished with rattan chairs and lounges in pale-green and pink, hung across with gauzy fabric.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThe_American_Architect_and_Building_News_Company18983_107-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_American_Architect_and_Building_News_Company18983-107">[106]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:left;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_building_2">Current building</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Waldorf_Astoria_New_York&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Current building">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Exterior">Exterior</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Waldorf_Astoria_New_York&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Exterior">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Waldorf_Astoria_exterior.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3416" data-file-width="2736" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Waldorf_Astoria_exterior.jpg/220px-Waldorf_Astoria_exterior.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Waldorf_Astoria_exterior.jpg/330px-Waldorf_Astoria_exterior.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Waldorf_Astoria_exterior.jpg/440px-Waldorf_Astoria_exterior.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Waldorf_Astoria_exterior.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Waldorf Astoria, Park Avenue facade</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Travel America</i> stated: "To linger in the sumptuous salons of the Waldorf-Astoria is to step back in time. Your trip down memory lane is a flashback to the glamor days of the 1930s, when this <a href="/wiki/Art_Deco" title="Art Deco">Art Deco</a> masterpiece was the tallest hotel in the world and the epicenter of elite society. A legendary limestone landmark occupying a whole block of prime real estate in midtown Manhattan, it's still a prestige address that embodies luxury and power in the richest city on earth."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup> The hotel was designed by architects <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Schultze_and_Weaver" title="Schultze and Weaver">Schultze and Weaver</a> and constructed at 301 <a href="/wiki/Park_Avenue" title="Park Avenue">Park Avenue</a>, just north of <a href="/wiki/Grand_Central_Terminal" title="Grand Central Terminal">Grand Central Terminal</a>. That area was developed by building atop the existing railroad tracks leading to the station, with buildings like the Waldorf Astoria utilizing "air rights" to the space above the tracks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapter_6:_Historic_Resources_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chapter_6:_Historic_Resources-112">[111]</a></sup> The new building opened on October 1, 1931. The 47-story 190.5 m (625 ft) hotel was the tallest and largest hotel in the world, and remained so for a number of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKorom2008422_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKorom2008422-47">[47]</a></sup> 1,585 cubic feet (44.9 m<sup>3</sup>) of black marble was imported from Belgium, 600 cubic feet (17 m<sup>3</sup>) of Brech Montalto and 260 cubic feet (7.4 m<sup>3</sup>) of Alps Green arrived from Italy, and some 300 antique mantles were brought in to furnish it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199153_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199153-113">[112]</a></sup> 200 railroad cars brought some 800,000 cubic feet (23,000 m<sup>3</sup>) of limestone for the building's facing, 27,100 tons of steel for the skeleton superstructure, and 2,595,000 square feet (241,100 m<sup>2</sup>) of terra cotta and gypsum block. The towers are brick-faced, which led many to believe that the builders ran out of money.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199153_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199153-113">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>Peacock Alley, a 300 feet (91 m) long corridor lined with amber marble connects the two hotel buildings. Gilded, women of the times would enjoy walking along it and admiring themselves in the mirrors. In 1931 it was reported that as many as 36,000 people were walking down it on any given day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELashleyLynchMorrison2007102_109-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELashleyLynchMorrison2007102-109">[108]</a></sup> The Peacock Alley restaurant of the Waldorf took its name from the alley.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991130_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991130-114">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>The hotel had its own <a href="/wiki/Railway_platform" title="Railway platform">railway platform</a>, <a href="/wiki/Track_61_(New_York_City)" title="Track 61 (New York City)">Track 61</a>, that was part of the <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Subway" title="New York City Subway">New York City Subway</a> and was connected to the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Central_Terminal" title="Grand Central Terminal">Grand Central Terminal</a> complex. The platform was used by <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Farley" title="James Farley">James Farley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adlai_Stevenson_II" title="Adlai Stevenson II">Adlai Stevenson</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Douglas_MacArthur" title="Douglas MacArthur">Douglas MacArthur</a>, among others. The platform was also used for the exhibition of <a href="/wiki/American_Locomotive_Company" title="American Locomotive Company">American Locomotive Company</a>'s new diesel locomotive in 1946. In 1948, <a href="/wiki/Filene%27s" title="Filene's">Filene's</a> and the <a href="/wiki/New_York,_New_Haven_and_Hartford_Railroad" title="New York, New Haven and Hartford Railroad">New York, New Haven and Hartford Railroad</a> also staged a fashion show on the platform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brennanjoe_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-brennanjoe-115">[114]</a></sup> An elevator large enough for Franklin D. Roosevelt's automobile provides access to the platform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt_20141007_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-nyt_20141007-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup> However, it is rarely opened to the public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brennanjoe_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-brennanjoe-115">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Interior">Interior</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Waldorf_Astoria_New_York&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Interior">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tmulti tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:254px;max-width:254px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Waldorf_Astoria,_NY.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1350" data-file-width="1800" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Waldorf_Astoria%2C_NY.jpg/250px-Waldorf_Astoria%2C_NY.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Waldorf_Astoria%2C_NY.jpg/375px-Waldorf_Astoria%2C_NY.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Waldorf_Astoria%2C_NY.jpg/500px-Waldorf_Astoria%2C_NY.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Waldorf_Astoria_foyer.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2344" data-file-width="3527" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Waldorf_Astoria_foyer.jpg/250px-Waldorf_Astoria_foyer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Waldorf_Astoria_foyer.jpg/375px-Waldorf_Astoria_foyer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Waldorf_Astoria_foyer.jpg/500px-Waldorf_Astoria_foyer.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Above: The main registration lobby. Below: The foyer</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Such is the architectural and cultural heritage of the hotel that tours are conducted of the hotel for guests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-fact-25">[25]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Frommer%27s" title="Frommer's">Frommer's</a> has cited the hotel as an "icon of luxury", and highlights the "wide stately corridors, the vintage Deco door fixtures, the white-gloved bellmen, the luxe shopping arcade", the "stunning round mosaic under an immense crystal chandelier" and the "free-standing Waldorf clock, covered with bronze relief figures" in the main lobby. They compare the decor of the rooms to those of an English country house, and describe the corridors as being wide and plush-carpeted which "seem to go on forever".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlippin201134_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlippin201134-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>The lobby floor contains the room registration and cashier desks, the Empire Room and Hilton Room, the private Marco Polo Club, the Wedding Salon, Kenneth's Salon, the <i>Peacock Alley</i> lounge and restaurant, and <i>Sir Harry's Bar</i>. From 1992 to 2013, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kenneth_(hairdresser)" title="Kenneth (hairdresser)">Kenneth</a>, sometimes called the world's first celebrity hairdresser,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReed201225_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReed201225-120">[119]</a></sup> famed for creating <a href="/wiki/Jacqueline_Kennedy_Onassis" title="Jacqueline Kennedy Onassis">Jacqueline Kennedy</a>'s bouffant in 1961,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWong2010151_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWong2010151-121">[120]</a></sup> moved his hairdressing and beauty salon to the Waldorf after a 1990 fire destroyed his East 54th Street shop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup> In the main foyer is a chandelier measuring 10 feet (3.0 m) by 10 feet (3.0 m). The elevator is furnished with paneled pollard oak and Carpathian elm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991141_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991141-123">[122]</a></sup> Special desks in the lobby are allocated to transportation and theatre, where exclusive tickets to many of the city's prominent theatres can be purchased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-fact-25">[25]</a></sup> The lobby is furnished with polished nickel-bronze cornices and rockwood stone. The grand clock, a 4,000 pounds (1,800 kg) bronze was built by the Goldsmith's Company of London originally for the 1893 World Columbia Exposition in Chicago, but was purchased by the Waldorf owners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199154_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199154-124">[123]</a></sup> Its base is octagonal, with eight commemorative plaques of presidents <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Ulysses S. Grant">Ulysses S. Grant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Harrison" title="Benjamin Harrison">Benjamin Harrison</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grover_Cleveland" title="Grover Cleveland">Grover Cleveland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">Benjamin Franklin</a>. A shield once belonging to the Waldorf was moved to the <i>Alexis</i> restaurant on W. Franklin Street in 1984.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[124]</a></sup> Several boutiques surround the lobby, which contains <a href="/wiki/Cole_Porter" title="Cole Porter">Cole Porter</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Steinway_%26_Sons" title="Steinway &amp; Sons">Steinway &amp; Sons</a> floral print decorated grand piano on the Cocktail Terrace, which the hotel had once given him as a gift.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavis201127_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavis201127-126">[125]</a></sup> Porter was a resident at the hotel for 30 years and composed many of his songs here.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[126]</a></sup> The Empire Room is where many of the musical and dance performances were put on, from <a href="/wiki/Count_Basie" title="Count Basie">Count Basie</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Victor_Borge" title="Victor Borge">Victor Borge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gordon_MacRae" title="Gordon MacRae">Gordon MacRae</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_M._Cohan" title="George M. Cohan">George M. Cohan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lena_Horne" title="Lena Horne">Lena Horne</a>, the first black performer at the hotel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199160.E2.80.931_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199160.E2.80.931-128">[127]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:408px;max-width:408px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Waldolfastoriaelevatorlobby.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="960" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Waldolfastoriaelevatorlobby.JPG/200px-Waldolfastoriaelevatorlobby.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Waldolfastoriaelevatorlobby.JPG/300px-Waldolfastoriaelevatorlobby.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Waldolfastoriaelevatorlobby.JPG/400px-Waldolfastoriaelevatorlobby.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Waldorf_Astoria_lobby_view_2.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="2736" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Waldorf_Astoria_lobby_view_2.jpg/200px-Waldorf_Astoria_lobby_view_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Waldorf_Astoria_lobby_view_2.jpg/300px-Waldorf_Astoria_lobby_view_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Waldorf_Astoria_lobby_view_2.jpg/400px-Waldorf_Astoria_lobby_view_2.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Left: The elevator lobby. Right: The Park Avenue lobby.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The third floor contains the Grand Ballroom, the Silver Corridor, the Basildon Room, the Jade Room and the Astor Gallery. Numerous organizations hold their annual dinners in the grand ballroom of the Waldorf, including <a href="/wiki/St._John%27s_University_(New_York_City)" title="St. John's University (New York City)">St. John's University</a> President's Dinner,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[128]</a></sup> the Catholic Charities of the Archdiocese of New York's annual gala, during which the Deus Caritas Est Award for philanthropy is presented, and the <a href="/wiki/Alfred_E._Smith_Memorial_Foundation_Dinner" title="Alfred E. Smith Memorial Foundation Dinner">Alfred E. Smith Memorial Foundation Dinner</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/NASCAR" title="NASCAR">NASCAR</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sprint_Cup_Series" title="Sprint Cup Series">Sprint Cup</a> end-of-season awards banquet was held at the Waldorf-Astoria every year between 1981 and 2008 before moving to the <a href="/wiki/Wynn_Las_Vegas" title="Wynn Las Vegas">Wynn</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Las_Vegas,_Nevada" title="Las Vegas, Nevada">Las Vegas</a>. It was held initially in the Starlight Room, but from 1985, it was staged in the Grand Ballroom, except in 2001 and 2002.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[130]</a></sup> On May 1, 2004, the Waldorf-Astoria was the venue for the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Hungarian_Network" title="Manhattan Hungarian Network">Manhattan Hungarian Network</a> Grand Europe Ball, a historic black-tie charitable affair co-chaired by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archduke_Georg_of_Austria-Hungary" title="Archduke Georg of Austria-Hungary">Archduke Georg of Austria-Hungary</a> which celebrated the <a href="/wiki/Enlargement_of_the_European_Union" title="Enlargement of the European Union">Enlargement of the European Union</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bob_Hope" title="Bob Hope">Bob Hope</a> was such a regular performer at the Ballroom that he said, "I've played so many dinners in the Grand Ballroom, I always make a crack when I get up to speak that I leave my dinner jacket in the lobby so that I don't have to ship it to the Coast all the time".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991254_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991254-79">[78]</a></sup> Of note in the Astor Gallery are 12 allegorical females, painted by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edward_Emerson_Simmons" title="Edward Emerson Simmons">Edward Emerson Simmons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199155_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199155-133">[132]</a></sup> Every October the Paris Ball was held in the Grand Ballroom, before moving to the Americana (now the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sheraton_Center" title="Sheraton Center">Sheraton Center</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991140_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991140-134">[133]</a></sup> It hosted a memorable New Year's Eve party with <a href="/wiki/Guy_Lombardo" title="Guy Lombardo">Guy Lombardo</a> and the Royal Canadians, and Lombardo used to broadcast live on the radio there from the "Starlight Roof". <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Chevalier" title="Maurice Chevalier">Maurice Chevalier</a> performed at the ballroom in 1965 in his last appearance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199164-5_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199164-5-135">[134]</a></sup> The Silver Corridor outside the ballroom bears a resemblance to the Peacock Alley, but is shorter and wider.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991142_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991142-136">[135]</a></sup> The fourth floor has the banquet and sales offices, and many of the suites including <a href="/wiki/Blue_Barron" title="Blue Barron">Barron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cornelius_Vanderbilt_IV" title="Cornelius Vanderbilt IV">Vanderbilt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Windsor" title="Duke of Windsor">Windsor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Conrad_Hilton" title="Conrad Hilton">Conrad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marcel_Vert%C3%A8s" title="Marcel Vertès">Vertès</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louis_XVI_of_France" title="Louis XVI of France">Louis XVI</a> and Cole Porter, named after the celebrities who stayed in them. The fourth floor was where the notorious Sunday night card games were played.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199196_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199196-137">[136]</a></sup> There is also a re-creation of one of the living rooms of Hoover's Waldorf-Astoria suite in the <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover_Presidential_Library_and_Museum" title="Herbert Hoover Presidential Library and Museum">Herbert Hoover Presidential Library and Museum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc_20090922_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-abc_20090922-138">[137]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Rooms_and_suites">Rooms and suites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Waldorf_Astoria_New_York&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Rooms and suites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Waldorf_Towers_suite_30A.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1440" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Waldorf_Towers_suite_30A.jpg/250px-Waldorf_Towers_suite_30A.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Waldorf_Towers_suite_30A.jpg/375px-Waldorf_Towers_suite_30A.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Waldorf_Towers_suite_30A.jpg/500px-Waldorf_Towers_suite_30A.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Waldorf_Towers_suite_30A.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Waldorf Towers suite 30A</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Waldorf Astoria and Towers has a total of 1,413 hotel rooms as of 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt_20141007_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-nyt_20141007-1">[1]</a></sup> In 2009, when it had 1,416 rooms, the main hotel had 1,235 single and double rooms and 208 mini suites, 17 of which were classified as "Astoria Level", which are upgraded rooms with deluxe amenities and complimentary access to the Astoria Lounge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_25-3"><a href="#cite_note-fact-25">[25]</a></sup> The Waldorf Towers, from the 28th floor up to the 42nd, had 181 rooms, of which 115 were suites, with one to four bedrooms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_25-4"><a href="#cite_note-fact-25">[25]</a></sup> As of the late 1990s, the hotel had a housekeeping staff of nearly 400, with 150-day maids and two dozen night maids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991187_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991187-139">[138]</a></sup> The rooms retain the original Art Deco motifs, although each room is decorated differently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_25-5"><a href="#cite_note-fact-25">[25]</a></sup> The guests rooms, classified as Deluxe, Superior, and Luxury, feature "Waldorf Serenity" beds and have a marble bath or shower with amenities designed by <a href="/wiki/Salvatore_Ferragamo" title="Salvatore Ferragamo">Salvatore Ferragamo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[139]</a></sup> The suites featured King or Double beds and start in size at 450 square feet (42 m<sup>2</sup>). The smallest are the One Bedroom suites, which range from 450 square feet (42 m<sup>2</sup>) to 600 square feet (56 m<sup>2</sup>), then there are the Signature suites, with a separate living room and one or two bedrooms, which range from 750 square feet (70 m<sup>2</sup>) to 900 square feet (84 m<sup>2</sup>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[140]</a></sup> and finally the suites of The Towers which are generally larger and costlier still, and have a twice-daily maid service.</p>
<p>The Tower suites are divided into standard ones, The Towers Luxury Series, which have their own sitting room, the Towers Penthouse Series, the Towers Presidential-Style Suites, and finally the most expensive Presidential Suite on the 35th floor. The Penthouse Series contains three suites, The Penthouse, The Cole Porter Suite, and The Royal Suite, named after the Duke and Duchess of Windsor. They start at 1,800 square feet (170 m<sup>2</sup>) in size, with two or more bedrooms, and are fitted with a kitchen and dining room which can accommodate for 8-12 guests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Towers_suites_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Towers_suites-142">[141]</a></sup> The Towers Presidential-Style Suites are divided into the MacArthur Suite and the Churchill Suite, and have their own grand entry foyer. Like the Penthouse Series, they have their own kitchen and dining room.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Towers_suites_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-Towers_suites-142">[141]</a></sup> The 2,250 square feet (209 m<sup>2</sup>) Presidential Suite is designed with Georgian-style furniture to emulate that of the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Towers_suites_142-2"><a href="#cite_note-Towers_suites-142">[141]</a></sup> It has three large bedrooms and three bathrooms, and boasts numerous treasures, including the desk of General MacArthur and rocking chair of John F. Kennedy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991151_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991151-143">[142]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_facilities">Other facilities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Waldorf_Astoria_New_York&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Other facilities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>A 2,500 square feet (230 m<sup>2</sup>) fitness center is on the 5th floor. The $21.5 million Waldorf Astoria Guerlain Spa was inaugurated on September 1, 2008 on the 19th floor. It features 16 treatment rooms and two relaxation lounges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_25-6"><a href="#cite_note-fact-25">[25]</a></sup> The hotel has its own Business Center, a 1,150 square feet (107 m<sup>2</sup>) digital facility, where guests can access the Internet and photocopy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_25-7"><a href="#cite_note-fact-25">[25]</a></sup> In 2004 the hotel launched a line of products in keeping with the Art Deco style of the hotel, reportedly becoming the first individual hotel in the world to have its own merchandise collection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[143]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Secret_railway_track">Secret railway track</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Waldorf_Astoria_New_York&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Secret railway track">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Underneath the hotel is a private railway track and platform directly connected to the Grand Central Terminal, referred to as <a href="/wiki/Track_61_(New_York_City)" title="Track 61 (New York City)">Track 61</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[145]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Restaurants_and_cuisine">Restaurants and cuisine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Waldorf_Astoria_New_York&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Restaurants and cuisine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Waldorf-Astoria_menu_Archbishop_of_York_dinner.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1978" data-file-width="1422" height="306" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Waldorf-Astoria_menu_Archbishop_of_York_dinner.jpg/220px-Waldorf-Astoria_menu_Archbishop_of_York_dinner.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Waldorf-Astoria_menu_Archbishop_of_York_dinner.jpg/330px-Waldorf-Astoria_menu_Archbishop_of_York_dinner.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Waldorf-Astoria_menu_Archbishop_of_York_dinner.jpg/440px-Waldorf-Astoria_menu_Archbishop_of_York_dinner.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Waldorf-Astoria_menu_Archbishop_of_York_dinner.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1918 menu for a dinner honoring <a href="/wiki/Cosmo_Gordon_Lang" title="Cosmo Gordon Lang">Cosmo Gordon Lang</a>, Archbishop of York</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Waldorf Astoria was the first hotel to offer <a href="/wiki/Room_service" title="Room service">room service</a> and was the first major hotel in the world to hire women as chefs, beginning in 1931.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHilton1957227_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHilton1957227-147">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[147]</a></sup> An extensive menu is available for guests, with special menus for children and for dieters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_25-8"><a href="#cite_note-fact-25">[25]</a></sup> The executive chef of the Waldorf for many years was <a href="/wiki/John_Doherty_(chef)" title="John Doherty (chef)">John Doherty</a>, following the Austrian <a href="/wiki/Arno_Schmidt_(chef)" title="Arno Schmidt (chef)">Arno Schmidt</a> who held the position for ten years from 1969 to 1979.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavis201127_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavis201127-126">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991206_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991206-149">[148]</a></sup> Restaurateur George Lang was awarded the Hotelman of the Year Award in 1975.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991119_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991119-150">[149]</a></sup> As of the early 1990s, the hotel served over three million dishes a year, and got through 27,000 pounds of lobster, 100 pounds of beluga caviar, 380,000 pints of strawberries annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991204_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991204-151">[150]</a></sup> The hotel has gained significant renown for its lavish feasts. During one grand feast for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Francis_Cardinal_Spellman" title="Francis Cardinal Spellman">Francis Cardinal Spellman</a>, over 200 VIP guests, according to Arno Schmidt, devoured some 3,600 pounds of fillet, 600 pounds of fresh halibut, 1,500 pounds of potatoes, and 260 pounds of petit fours, eating on gold china plates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991208.E2.80.939_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991208.E2.80.939-152">[151]</a></sup> One 1973 feast by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Explorer%27s_Club" title="Explorer's Club">Explorer's Club</a> devoured hippo meat, a 4-foot (1.2 m) alligator, a baby shark, an amberjack tuna, a boa, wild boar hams, 480 pieces of breaded-fried cod tongues and cheeks, antelope steaks, two boxes of Chinese rabbit, and 20 pounds of rattlesnake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991222_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991222-153">[152]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Waldorf_Astoria_buffet.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Waldorf_Astoria_buffet.jpg/250px-Waldorf_Astoria_buffet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Waldorf_Astoria_buffet.jpg/375px-Waldorf_Astoria_buffet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Waldorf_Astoria_buffet.jpg/500px-Waldorf_Astoria_buffet.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Waldorf_Astoria_buffet.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Breakfast at the Waldorf Astoria</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The hotel has three main restaurants, <i>Peacock Alley</i>, <i>The Bull and Bear Steak House</i>, and <i>Oscar's Brasserie</i>, as well as a secondary restaurant, the Japanese <i>Inagiku</i>. At its peak in the late 1940s, the hotel once had nine restaurants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991210_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991210-154">[153]</a></sup> <i>Peacock Alley</i>, situated in the heart of the lobby, features an Art Deco design with gilded ceilings and includes a main restaurant, a bar and lounge, and three private dining salons. It is known primarily for its fish and seafood dishes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_25-9"><a href="#cite_note-fact-25">[25]</a></sup> Sunday Brunch is particularly popular with locals and features over 180 gourmet dishes divided into 12 themed displays, with cuisine ranging from lobster and oysters to Belgian waffles, Eggs Benedict, and omelettes to hollandaise sauces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_25-10"><a href="#cite_note-fact-25">[25]</a></sup> <i>The Bull and Bear Steak House</i> is furnished in richly polished mahogany in the English Regency style,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991200_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991200-155">[154]</a></sup> and has a "den-like" atmosphere,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III19918_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III19918-23">[23]</a></sup> and is reportedly the only restaurant on the East Coast which serves 28 days prime grade USDA Certified Angus Beef.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_25-11"><a href="#cite_note-fact-25">[25]</a></sup> It has won awards from the <a href="/wiki/National_Restaurant_Association" title="National Restaurant Association">National Restaurant Association</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Holiday_(magazine)" title="Holiday (magazine)">Holiday</a></i> magazine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991199_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991199-156">[155]</a></sup> Between 2007 and 2010, the restaurant was the filming location for <i><a href="/wiki/Fox_Business_Happy_Hour" title="Fox Business Happy Hour">Fox Business Happy Hour</a></i>, presented live between 5 and 6 pm. The <i>Bull and Bear Bar</i> is based on the original Waldorf Astoria Bar, which was a favorite haunt of many of the financial elite of the city from the hotel's inception in 1893, such as <a href="/wiki/Diamond_Jim_Brady" title="Diamond Jim Brady">Diamond Jim Brady</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buffalo_Bill_Cody" title="Buffalo Bill Cody">Buffalo Bill Cody</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bat_Masterson" title="Bat Masterson">Bat Masterson</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dining_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dining-157">[156]</a></sup> Behind the bar are bronze statues of a bull and a bear, which represent the successful men of <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street" title="Wall Street">Wall Street</a>. The <i>Inagiku</i>, meaning the "rice chrysanthemum",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991211_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991211-158">[157]</a></sup> serves contemporary Japanese cuisine. The restaurant opens for lunch on weekdays and cocktails and dinner in the evenings. Designed by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_Look&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Henry Look (page does not exist)">Henry Look</a> of San Francisco, the restaurant has four "distinctly different" rooms, including one which represents an old Japanese farmhouse, and the Kinagu Room, resembling a Japanese temple.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991211_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991211-158">[157]</a></sup> Guests have the option to reserve private orthodox <a href="/wiki/Tatami" title="Tatami">tatami</a> rooms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_25-12"><a href="#cite_note-fact-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Waldorfsalat.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1896" data-file-width="2109" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Waldorfsalat.jpg/220px-Waldorfsalat.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Waldorfsalat.jpg/330px-Waldorfsalat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Waldorfsalat.jpg/440px-Waldorfsalat.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Waldorfsalat.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The classic Waldorf Salad</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Oscar's Brasserie</i>, overlooking Lexington Avenue in what was once a Savarin restaurant, is designed by <a href="/wiki/Adam_Tihany" title="Adam Tihany">Adam Tihany</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_25-13"><a href="#cite_note-fact-25">[25]</a></sup> The restaurant takes its name from Oscar Tschirky (Oscar of the Waldorf) and serves traditional American cuisine, with many dishes based upon his cookbook which have gained world renown, including the Waldorf salad, Eggs Benedict, Thousand Island dressing, and Veal Oscar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_25-14"><a href="#cite_note-fact-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorrison2014121_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorrison2014121-159">[158]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Waldorf_salad" title="Waldorf salad">Waldorf salad</a>—a salad made with <a href="/wiki/Apple" title="Apple">apples</a>, <a href="/wiki/Walnut" title="Walnut">walnuts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Celery" title="Celery">celery</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grapes" title="Grapes">grapes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mayonnaise" title="Mayonnaise">mayonnaise</a> or a mayonnaise-based dressing—was first created in 1896 at the Waldorf by Oscar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[159]</a></sup> The original recipe, however did not contain nuts, but they had been added by the time the recipe appeared in <i>The Rector Cook Book</i> in 1928. Tschirky was also noted for his "Oscar's Sauce", which became so popular that it was sold at the hotel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199124_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199124-161">[160]</a></sup> Another of the hotel's specialties was red velvet cake, which became one of its most popular desserts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFineTurner200110_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFineTurner200110-162">[161]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Sir Harry's Bar</i> is one of the principal bars of the hotel, situated just off the main lobby. It is named after British Sir <a href="/wiki/Harry_Johnston" title="Harry Johnston">Harry Johnston</a> (1858–1927). In the 1970s the bar was renovated in a "plush African safari" design to honor Johnston, a notable explorer of Africa, with "zebra-striped wall coverings and carpeting, with bent-cane furnishings".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorrison2014121_159-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorrison2014121-159">[158]</a></sup> It has since been redecorated back to a more conservative design, with walnut paneling and leather banquettes, and featured a 23 feet (7.0 m) by 8 feet (2.4 m) ebony bar as of the early 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991211_158-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991211-158">[157]</a></sup> A number of cocktails were invented at the bar, including the <a href="/wiki/Rob_Roy_(cocktail)" title="Rob Roy (cocktail)">Rob Roy</a> (1894) and the <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Burns_(drink)" title="Bobby Burns (drink)">Bobbie Burns</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_25-15"><a href="#cite_note-fact-25">[25]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Frank_Sinatra" title="Frank Sinatra">Frank Sinatra</a> frequented <i>Sir Harry's Bar</i> for many years. In 1991, while drinking at <i>Sir Harry's</i> with <a href="/wiki/Jilly_Rizzo" title="Jilly Rizzo">Jilly Rizzo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steve_Lawrence" title="Steve Lawrence">Steve Lawrence</a>, he was approached by a fan asking for an autograph. Sinatra responded, "Don't you see I'm on my own time here? You asshole. What's wrong with you?" The fan said something which angered Sinatra, who lunged at the fan, and Sinatra had to be restrained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERojek2004141_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERojek2004141-163">[162]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_residents_and_tenants">Notable residents and tenants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Waldorf_Astoria_New_York&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Notable residents and tenants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leaders_and_businesspeople">Leaders and businesspeople</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Waldorf_Astoria_New_York&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Leaders and businesspeople">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eleanor_Roosevelt_and_the_Royal_Family_of_Norway_with_Princess_Juliana_of_the_Netherlands_and_Thomas_Watson_-_NARA_-_195657.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2360" data-file-width="3000" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Eleanor_Roosevelt_and_the_Royal_Family_of_Norway_with_Princess_Juliana_of_the_Netherlands_and_Thomas_Watson_-_NARA_-_195657.jpg/220px-Eleanor_Roosevelt_and_the_Royal_Family_of_Norway_with_Princess_Juliana_of_the_Netherlands_and_Thomas_Watson_-_NARA_-_195657.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Eleanor_Roosevelt_and_the_Royal_Family_of_Norway_with_Princess_Juliana_of_the_Netherlands_and_Thomas_Watson_-_NARA_-_195657.jpg/330px-Eleanor_Roosevelt_and_the_Royal_Family_of_Norway_with_Princess_Juliana_of_the_Netherlands_and_Thomas_Watson_-_NARA_-_195657.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Eleanor_Roosevelt_and_the_Royal_Family_of_Norway_with_Princess_Juliana_of_the_Netherlands_and_Thomas_Watson_-_NARA_-_195657.jpg/440px-Eleanor_Roosevelt_and_the_Royal_Family_of_Norway_with_Princess_Juliana_of_the_Netherlands_and_Thomas_Watson_-_NARA_-_195657.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eleanor_Roosevelt_and_the_Royal_Family_of_Norway_with_Princess_Juliana_of_the_Netherlands_and_Thomas_Watson_-_NARA_-_195657.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Eleanor Roosevelt and the Crown Prince and Crown Princess of Norway with Princess Juliana and Thomas Watson</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On the 100th anniversary of the hotel in 1993, one publication wrote: "It isn't the biggest hotel in New York, nor the most expensive. But when it comes to prestige, the Waldorf-Astoria has no peer. When presidents come to New York, they stay at the Waldorf-Astoria. Kings and queens make it their home away from home, as have people as diverse as <a href="/wiki/Cary_Grant" title="Cary Grant">Cary Grant</a>, the <a href="/wiki/14th_Dalai_Lama" title="14th Dalai Lama">Dalai Lama</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chris_Evert" title="Chris Evert">Chris Evert</a>. Some of them liked the hotel so well, they made their home there."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[163]</a></sup> From its inception, the Waldorf was always a "must stay" hotel for foreign dignitaries. The viceroy of China, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Li_Hung_Chang" title="Li Hung Chang">Li Hung Chang</a> stayed at the hotel in 1896 and feasted on hundred-year-old eggs which he brought with him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199121_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199121-20">[20]</a></sup> Over the years many royals from around the world stayed at the Waldorf Astoria including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shahanshah" title="Shahanshah">Shahanshah</a> of Iran and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empress_Farah" title="Empress Farah">Empress Farah</a>, King <a href="/wiki/Frederick_IX_of_Denmark" title="Frederick IX of Denmark">Frederick IX</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen_Ingrid_of_Denmark" title="Queen Ingrid of Denmark">Queen Ingrid of Denmark</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Princess_Astrid_of_Norway" title="Princess Astrid of Norway">Princess Astrid of Norway</a>, Crown Prince <a href="/wiki/Olav_V_of_Norway" title="Olav V of Norway">Olav</a> and Crown Princess <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Princess_Martha_of_Norway" title="Princess Martha of Norway">Martha of Norway</a>, King <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baudouin_I_of_Belgium" title="Baudouin I of Belgium">Baudouin I of Belgium</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen_Fabiola" title="Queen Fabiola">Queen Fabiola</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prince_Albert" title="Prince Albert">Prince Albert</a> and <a href="/wiki/Queen_Paola_of_Belgium" title="Queen Paola of Belgium">Princess Paola</a> of Belgium, King <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hussein_I" title="Hussein I">Hussein I</a> of Jordan, Prince <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rainier_III" title="Rainier III">Rainier III</a> and Princess <a href="/wiki/Grace_Kelly" title="Grace Kelly">Grace</a> of Monaco, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen_Juliana" title="Queen Juliana">Queen Juliana</a> of the Netherlands, King <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Michael_of_Romania" title="Michael of Romania">Michael of Romania</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_II" title="Queen Elizabeth II">Queen Elizabeth II</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prince_Philip" title="Prince Philip">Prince Philip</a> of the United Kingdom and Commonwealth realms, <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Zahir_Shah" title="Mohammed Zahir Shah">Mohammed Zahir Shah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Humaira_Begum" title="Humaira Begum">Homaira Shah</a> of Afghanistan, King <a href="/wiki/Bhumibol_Adulyadej" title="Bhumibol Adulyadej">Bhumibol Adulyadej</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sirikit" title="Sirikit">Queen Sirikit</a> of Thailand, and Crown Prince <a href="/wiki/Akihito" title="Akihito">Akihito</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Princess_Michiko" title="Princess Michiko">Princess Michiko</a> of Japan and many others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991106_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991106-165">[164]</a></sup> Queen Elizabeth II and Prince Philip stayed at the hotel during their first visit to America on October 21, 1957, and a banquet was held for them in the Grand Ballroom. In the Bicentennial year in 1976, most of the heads of state from around the world and all of the Kings and Queens of Europe were invited to the hotel, and it also served the presidential candidates in the run up to the elections of that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991117.2C_181_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991117.2C_181-166">[165]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Khrushchev_family_at_the_Waldorf-Astoria_1959.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="909" data-file-width="1478" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Khrushchev_family_at_the_Waldorf-Astoria_1959.jpg/250px-Khrushchev_family_at_the_Waldorf-Astoria_1959.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Khrushchev_family_at_the_Waldorf-Astoria_1959.jpg/375px-Khrushchev_family_at_the_Waldorf-Astoria_1959.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Khrushchev_family_at_the_Waldorf-Astoria_1959.jpg/500px-Khrushchev_family_at_the_Waldorf-Astoria_1959.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Khrushchev_family_at_the_Waldorf-Astoria_1959.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Khrushchev family at the Waldorf Astoria in 1959</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In modern times, the clientele of the Waldorf is more typically wealthy politicians and businessmen than playboys and royalty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991147_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991147-167">[166]</a></sup> An entire floor was often rented out to wealthy Saudi Arabians with their own staff. Wealthy Japanese businessmen during their stay would sometimes remove the furniture and replace it with their own floors mats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991142_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991142-136">[135]</a></sup> One early wealthy resident was Chicago businessman <a href="/wiki/John_Warne_Gates" title="John Warne Gates">J. W. Gates</a> who would gamble on stocks on Wall Street and play poker at the hotel. He paid up to $50,000 a year to hire suites at the hotel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199122_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199122-168">[167]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Princess_Victoria_Melita_of_Saxe-Coburg_and_Gotha" title="Princess Victoria Melita of Saxe-Coburg and Gotha">Princess Victoria Melita of Saxe-Coburg and Gotha</a> was invited by Waldorf president Lucius Bloomer to stay at the hotel in the 1920s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199127_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199127-169">[168]</a></sup> Demands by people of prominence could often be exorbitant or bizarre, and <a href="/wiki/Fidel_Castro" title="Fidel Castro">Fidel Castro</a> once walked into the hotel with a flock of live chickens, insisting that they be killed and freshly cooked on the premises to his satisfaction, only to be turned away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991168_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991168-170">[169]</a></sup> While serving as Secretary of State, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Kissinger" title="Henry Kissinger">Henry Kissinger</a> ordered all of the antiques to be removed from one suite and replaced them with 36 desks for his staff. An unnamed First Lady also once demanded that all of the bulbs in her suite be changed to 100 watt ones and kept on all day and night to simulate daylight; she further insisted that there be an abundance of chewing gum available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991161_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991161-171">[170]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HerbertHoover.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1131" data-file-width="912" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/HerbertHoover.jpg/170px-HerbertHoover.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/HerbertHoover.jpg/255px-HerbertHoover.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/HerbertHoover.jpg/340px-HerbertHoover.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HerbertHoover.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Herbert Hoover who lived at the Waldorf for over 30 years from the end of his presidency</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/United_States_Postmaster_General" title="United States Postmaster General">Postmaster General</a> <a href="/wiki/James_Farley" title="James Farley">James Farley</a> occupied two adjoining suites in the current Waldorf Astoria during his tenure as the chairman of the board of Coca-Cola's International division from 1940 until his death in 1976, arguably one of the landmark's longest housed tenants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[171]</a></sup> The Presidential Suite at the hotel come from when, during the 1950s and early 1960s, former U.S. president Herbert Hoover and retired U.S. General <a href="/wiki/Douglas_MacArthur" title="Douglas MacArthur">Douglas MacArthur</a> lived in suites on different floors of the hotel. Hoover lived at the Waldorf Astoria for over 30 years from after the end of his presidency<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc_20090922_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-abc_20090922-138">[137]</a></sup> until he died in 1964;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991151_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991151-143">[142]</a></sup> former President <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a> lived there until he died in 1969.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[172]</a></sup> MacArthur's widow, <a href="/wiki/Jean_MacArthur" title="Jean MacArthur">Jean MacArthur</a>, lived there from 1952 until her death in 2000. A plaque affixed to the wall on the 50th Street side commemorates this. John F. Kennedy was fond of the Waldorf Astoria and had a number of private meetings at the hotel, including one with Israeli Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/David_Ben-Gurion" title="David Ben-Gurion">David Ben-Gurion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHart2009406_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHart2009406-174">[173]</a></sup> Since Hoover, every <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a> has either stayed over or lived in the Waldorf Astoria, although <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a> claimed to have never stayed overnight at the hotel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc_20090922_138-2"><a href="#cite_note-abc_20090922-138">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991148_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991148-175">[174]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nancy_Reagan" title="Nancy Reagan">Nancy Reagan</a> was reputedly not fond of the Presidential Suite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199114_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199114-176">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199146.E2.80.937_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199146.E2.80.937-177">[176]</a></sup></p>
<p>The official residence of the United States' <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Permanent_Representative_to_the_UN" title="Permanent Representative to the UN">Permanent Representative</a> to the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, an unnamed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/42nd_Floor_Apartment" title="42nd Floor Apartment">42nd-floor apartment</a>, has been located in the Waldorf Towers for many years. On June 17, 2015, however, the US Department of State announced that it was moving its headquarters during meetings of the UN General Assembly to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_New_York_Palace_Hotel" title="The New York Palace Hotel">The New York Palace Hotel</a>. Although State Department officials did not give an official reason for the change affecting both the President and hundreds of American diplomats and support staff who travel to New York for the General Assembly each September and usually stay and hold meetings on two secured floors at the Waldorf, they pointed to Hilton Worldwide's sale of the Waldorf-Astoria to China's Beijing-based Anbang Insurance Group for $1.95 billion in 2014, a deal that prompted security concerns. While the terms of the sale allow Hilton to run the hotel for the next 100 years, they also call for "a major renovation" that the officials say has raised eyebrows in the US Government because of concerns about Chinese eavesdropping and cyber espionage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[177]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Carlos_P._Romulo" title="Carlos P. Romulo">Carlos P. Romulo</a>, Minister of Foreign Affairs of the Philippines and member of the UN had suite 3600, below Hoover's, for some 45 years from 1935 onwards, and fellow Filipino <a href="/wiki/Imelda_Marcos" title="Imelda Marcos">Imelda Marcos</a> also spent much time and money at the hotel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991151.E2.80.934_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991151.E2.80.934-179">[178]</a></sup> Another connection with the Philippines is that many meetings were held here between President <a href="/wiki/Manuel_L._Quezon" title="Manuel L. Quezon">Manuel L. Quezon</a> and high ranking American politicians and senators. Through the meetings, Quezon encouraged investment into the country and convinced General MacArthur to accompany him back to the Philippines as his military adviser.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991154_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991154-180">[179]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Celebrities">Celebrities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Waldorf_Astoria_New_York&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Celebrities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:395px;max-width:395px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Taylor,_Elizabeth_posed.jpg"><img alt="Elizabeth Taylor" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1255" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Taylor%2C_Elizabeth_posed.jpg/200px-Taylor%2C_Elizabeth_posed.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Taylor%2C_Elizabeth_posed.jpg/300px-Taylor%2C_Elizabeth_posed.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Taylor%2C_Elizabeth_posed.jpg/400px-Taylor%2C_Elizabeth_posed.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:189px;max-width:189px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Frank_Sinatra_1973.jpg"><img alt="Frank Sinatra" data-file-height="212" data-file-width="155" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1e/Frank_Sinatra_1973.jpg" width="187"/></a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Elizabeth Taylor and Frank Sinatra</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The hotel has had many well-known under its roof throughout its history, including <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Chaplin" title="Charlie Chaplin">Charlie Chaplin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ava_Gardner" title="Ava Gardner">Ava Gardner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liv_Ullmann" title="Liv Ullmann">Liv Ullmann</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edward_G._Robinson" title="Edward G. Robinson">Edward G. Robinson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gregory_Peck" title="Gregory Peck">Gregory Peck</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ray_Bolger" title="Ray Bolger">Ray Bolger</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Wayne" title="John Wayne">John Wayne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tony_Bennett" title="Tony Bennett">Tony Bennett</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jack_Benny" title="Jack Benny">Jack Benny</a>, <a href="/wiki/Katharine_Hepburn" title="Katharine Hepburn">Katharine Hepburn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spencer_Tracy" title="Spencer Tracy">Spencer Tracy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali" title="Muhammad Ali">Muhammad Ali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vince_Lombardi" title="Vince Lombardi">Vince Lombardi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Judy_Garland" title="Judy Garland">Judy Garland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sonny_Werblin" title="Sonny Werblin">Sonny Werblin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greer_Garson" title="Greer Garson">Greer Garson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harold_Lloyd" title="Harold Lloyd">Harold Lloyd</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liberace" title="Liberace">Liberace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Burt_Reynolds" title="Burt Reynolds">Burt Reynolds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Montgomery_(actor)" title="Robert Montgomery (actor)">Robert Montgomery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cesar_Romero" title="Cesar Romero">Cesar Romero</a>, and many others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199110.E2.80.932.2C_200.E2.80.931.2C_216.2C_252_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199110.E2.80.932.2C_200.E2.80.931.2C_216.2C_252-181">[180]</a></sup> Due to the number of high-profile guests staying at the hotel at any one time, author Ward Morehouse III has referred to the Towers as a "kind of vertical Beverly Hills. On any one given night you might find <a href="/wiki/Dinah_Shore" title="Dinah Shore">Dinah Shore</a>, Gregory Peck, Frank Sinatra [or] <a href="/wiki/Zsa_Zsa_Gabor" title="Zsa Zsa Gabor">Zsa Zsa Gabor</a> staying there".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991137_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991137-182">[181]</a></sup> Gabor married Conrad Hilton in 1941. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991147_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991147-167">[166]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the 1930s, gangster <a href="/wiki/Bugsy_Siegel" title="Bugsy Siegel">Benjamin "Bugsy" Siegel</a> owned an apartment at the Waldorf,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biography_of_a_Gangster_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-Biography_of_a_Gangster-183">[182]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Frank_Costello" title="Frank Costello">Frank Costello</a> was said to have got his haircut and nails done in the Barber's Shop at the Waldorf.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991254_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991254-79">[78]</a></sup> Around the time of World War I, inventor <a href="/wiki/Nikola_Tesla" title="Nikola Tesla">Nikola Tesla</a> lived in the earlier Waldorf-Astoria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[183]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1955, Marilyn Monroe stayed at the hotel for several months,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpoto1993337_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpoto1993337-185">[184]</a></sup> but due to costs of trying to finance her production company "Marilyn Monroe Productions", only being paid $1,500 a week for her role in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Seven_Year_Itch" title="The Seven Year Itch">The Seven Year Itch</a></i> and being suspended from <a href="/wiki/20th_Century_Fox" title="20th Century Fox">20th Century Fox</a> for walking out on Fox after creative differences, living at the hotel became too costly and Monroe had to move into a different hotel in New York City. Around the same time that Monroe lived in the hotel, Cole Porter and <a href="/wiki/Linda_Lee_Thomas" title="Linda Lee Thomas">Linda Lee Thomas</a> had an apartment in the Waldorf Towers, where Thomas died in 1954. Porter's 1934 song "<a href="/wiki/You%27re_the_Top" title="You're the Top">You're the Top</a>", contains the lyric, "You're the top, you're a Waldorf salad". The Cole Porter Suite, Suite 33A, was the place where Porter lived and entertained for a period. Frank Sinatra paid nearly $1 million a year to keep it as his personal suite at the hotel between 1979 and 1988, which he called "home" when out of Los Angeles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III19916.2C_146_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III19916.2C_146-186">[185]</a></sup> Sinatra took over part of the hotel during the filming of <i><a href="/wiki/The_First_Deadly_Sin" title="The First Deadly Sin">The First Deadly Sin</a></i> in 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991133_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991133-187">[186]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cast_from_Parks_and_Recreation,_Portlandia_and_Game_of_Thrones_at_the_71st_Annual_Peabody_Awards.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2832" data-file-width="4256" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Cast_from_Parks_and_Recreation%2C_Portlandia_and_Game_of_Thrones_at_the_71st_Annual_Peabody_Awards.jpg/250px-Cast_from_Parks_and_Recreation%2C_Portlandia_and_Game_of_Thrones_at_the_71st_Annual_Peabody_Awards.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Cast_from_Parks_and_Recreation%2C_Portlandia_and_Game_of_Thrones_at_the_71st_Annual_Peabody_Awards.jpg/375px-Cast_from_Parks_and_Recreation%2C_Portlandia_and_Game_of_Thrones_at_the_71st_Annual_Peabody_Awards.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Cast_from_Parks_and_Recreation%2C_Portlandia_and_Game_of_Thrones_at_the_71st_Annual_Peabody_Awards.jpg/500px-Cast_from_Parks_and_Recreation%2C_Portlandia_and_Game_of_Thrones_at_the_71st_Annual_Peabody_Awards.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cast_from_Parks_and_Recreation,_Portlandia_and_Game_of_Thrones_at_the_71st_Annual_Peabody_Awards.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The casts of <i><a href="/wiki/Parks_and_Recreation" title="Parks and Recreation">Parks and Recreation</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Portlandia_(TV_series)" title="Portlandia (TV series)">Portlandia</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Game_of_Thrones" title="Game of Thrones">Game of Thrones</a></i> at the 71st Annual Peabody Awards inside the Waldorf Astoria. From left to right: <a href="/wiki/Jim_O%27Heir" title="Jim O'Heir">Jim O'Heir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nick_Offerman" title="Nick Offerman">Nick Offerman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aziz_Ansari" title="Aziz Ansari">Aziz Ansari</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adam_Scott_(actor)" title="Adam Scott (actor)">Adam Scott</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carrie_Brownstein" title="Carrie Brownstein">Carrie Brownstein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nikolaj_Coster-Waldau" title="Nikolaj Coster-Waldau">Nikolaj Coster-Waldau</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amy_Poehler" title="Amy Poehler">Amy Poehler</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fred_Armisen" title="Fred Armisen">Fred Armisen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aubrey_Plaza" title="Aubrey Plaza">Aubrey Plaza</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Retta" title="Retta">Retta</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Grace Kelly and Rainier III were regular guests at the hotel. At one time Kelly was reputed to be in love with the hotel banquet manager of the Waldorf, Claudius Charles Philippe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991136_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991136-188">[187]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Taylor" title="Elizabeth Taylor">Elizabeth Taylor</a> frequented the hotel, and would often attend galas at the hotel to talk about her various causes. Her visits were excitedly awaited by the hotel staff, who would prepare long in advance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III19919_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III19919-189">[188]</a></sup> Taylor was honored at the 1983 Friars Club dinner at the hotel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991143_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991143-190">[189]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1980, <a href="/wiki/John_Travolta" title="John Travolta">John Travolta</a>'s brother <a href="/wiki/Joey_Travolta" title="Joey Travolta">Joey Travolta</a> and Wendy Shawn, the daughter of comedian <a href="/wiki/Dick_Shawn" title="Dick Shawn">Dick Shawn</a>, had their wedding reception at the hotel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991213_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991213-191">[190]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Brooke_Shields" title="Brooke Shields">Brooke Shields</a> has stated that her very first encounter with the paparazzi was in the Grand Ballroom of the Waldorf at the age of 12, stating that she "stood like a statue wondering why they were all hired to photograph me", and that she "debuted at the Waldorf". <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991254-5_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III1991254-5-192">[191]</a></sup> During her childhood in the 1980s and 1990s, <a href="/wiki/Paris_Hilton" title="Paris Hilton">Paris Hilton</a> lived with her family in the hotel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[192]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Waldorf_Astoria_New_York&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: In popular culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Waldorf Astoria has been a filming location for numerous films and TV series. <a href="/wiki/Ginger_Rogers" title="Ginger Rogers">Ginger Rogers</a> headlined an all star <a href="/wiki/Ensemble_cast" title="Ensemble cast">ensemble cast</a> in the 1945 film <i><a href="/wiki/Week-End_at_the_Waldorf" title="Week-End at the Waldorf">Week-End at the Waldorf</a></i>, set at the hotel and filmed partially on location there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_13-8"><a href="#cite_note-history-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnn_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-cnn-21">[21]</a></sup> Other films shot at the hotel include <i><a href="/wiki/The_Out-of-Towners_(1970_film)" title="The Out-of-Towners (1970 film)">The Out-of-Towners</a></i> (1970), <i><a href="/wiki/Broadway_Danny_Rose" title="Broadway Danny Rose">Broadway Danny Rose</a></i> (1984), <i><a href="/wiki/Coming_to_America" title="Coming to America">Coming to America</a></i> (1988), <i><a href="/wiki/Scent_of_a_Woman_(1992_film)" title="Scent of a Woman (1992 film)">Scent of a Woman</a></i> (1992), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Cowboy_Way_(film)" title="The Cowboy Way (film)">The Cowboy Way</a></i> (1994), <i><a href="/wiki/Random_Hearts" title="Random Hearts">Random Hearts</a></i> (1999), <i><a href="/wiki/Analyze_This" title="Analyze This">Analyze This</a></i> (1999), <i><a href="/wiki/For_Love_of_the_Game" title="For Love of the Game">For Love of the Game</a></i> (1999), <i><a href="/wiki/Serendipity_(film)" title="Serendipity (film)">Serendipity</a></i> (2001), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Royal_Tenenbaums" title="The Royal Tenenbaums">The Royal Tenenbaums</a></i> (2001), <i><a href="/wiki/Maid_in_Manhattan" title="Maid in Manhattan">Maid in Manhattan</a></i> (2002), <i><a href="/wiki/Two_Weeks_Notice" title="Two Weeks Notice">Two Weeks Notice</a></i> (2002), <i><a href="/wiki/End_of_the_Century" title="End of the Century">End of the Century</a></i> (2005), <i><a href="/wiki/Mr._%26_Mrs._Smith_(2005_film)" title="Mr. &amp; Mrs. Smith (2005 film)">Mr. and Mrs. Smith</a></i> (2005), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Pink_Panther_(2006_film)" title="The Pink Panther (2006 film)">The Pink Panther</a></i> (2006), and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hoax" title="The Hoax">The Hoax</a></i> (2006).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_25-16"><a href="#cite_note-fact-25">[25]</a></sup> Television series that have filmed at the Waldorf include <i><a href="/wiki/Law_%26_Order" title="Law &amp; Order">Law and Order</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Rescue_Me_(U.S._TV_series)" title="Rescue Me (U.S. TV series)">Rescue Me</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Sex_and_the_City" title="Sex and the City">Sex and the City</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sopranos" title="The Sopranos">The Sopranos</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Will_and_Grace" title="Will and Grace">Will and Grace</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_25-17"><a href="#cite_note-fact-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>Several biographies have been written about the Waldorf, including <a href="/wiki/Edward_Hungerford_(author)" title="Edward Hungerford (author)">Edward Hungerford</a>'s <i>Story of the Waldorf</i> (New York: G. P. Putnam's Sons, 1925) and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Horace_Sutton&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Horace Sutton (page does not exist)">Horace Sutton</a>'s <i>Confessions of a Grand Hotel: The Waldorf-Astoria</i> (New York: Henry Holt, 1953).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWharton2001199_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWharton2001199-194">[193]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Langston_Hughes" title="Langston Hughes">Langston Hughes</a> wrote a poem entitled "Advertisement for the Waldorf-Astoria", criticizing the hotel and inviting the jobless and homeless to take over the space of the hotel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHughes2008_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHughes2008-195">[194]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wallace_Stevens" title="Wallace Stevens">Wallace Stevens</a> wrote a poem entitled "Arrival at the Waldorf", in which he contrasts the wild country of the jungles of <a href="/wiki/Guatemala" title="Guatemala">Guatemala</a> to being "back at the Waldorf". In <a href="/wiki/Meg_Cabot" title="Meg Cabot">Meg Cabot</a>'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/Jinx_(Cabot_novel)" title="Jinx (Cabot novel)">Jinx</a></i>, the Chapman School Spring Formal takes place in the Waldorf-Astoria. It is at this point that Tory (the main antagonist) reveals Jean's first attempt at a love spell, which served as a catalyst for the novel's events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECabot2008191_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECabot2008191-196">[195]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Waldorf_Astoria_New_York&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Waldorf_Astoria_New_York&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><b>Explanatory notes</b></p>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-buy-66"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-buy_66-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Professor Elazar L. Sukenik was first offered the scrolls in 1947 by an antiquities dealer in Bethlehem. Because of the recent partitioning of Palestine, Jews were not permitted to travel there. Sukenik disguised himself as an Arab to travel to the city. He was allowed to examine the scrolls and to take a small fragment of one for testing. When he made the trip back to Bethlehem to purchase them the next day, he found that the dealer had been pressured into selling them to the Syriac Orthodox Church. Archbishop Mar Samuel offered to sell them to Sukenik for US$125,000; before the transaction could take place, Mar Samuel's life was threatened and he fled to the United States. Sukenik died in 1953 without further word about the whereabouts of the scrolls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scrolls_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-scrolls-64">[64]</a></sup> In 1954, a classified ad appeared in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal" title="The Wall Street Journal">The Wall Street Journal</a></i> offering to sell the four scrolls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Yigael Yadin, the son of Professor Sukenik, was visiting the United States when the ad appeared and someone brought it to his attention. It was then planned that the State of Israel would secretly buy the scrolls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scrolls_64-2"><a href="#cite_note-scrolls-64">[64]</a></sup></span></li>
</ol>
</div>
